The Sunni Definition of Guidance: Indication, Not Arrival
Commentary on the poet's line defining guidance and interpreting the hadith of companions as stars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Sunni definition of guidance (al-hidāya) and distinguish it from the Mu'tazili definition, using the scriptural evidence provided in the passage.

Core Idea
Guidance is showing the path; reaching the goal is a separate gift from Allah.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani's poem clarifies the meaning of 'guidance' in the line 'ذوي الهُدَى' and defends the Sunni position against a Mu'tazili objection. Understanding this definition is crucial for theology and tafsir. We will dissect the arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical role of 'ذوي الهُدَى'
In the poem, 'ذوي الهُدَى' is an attribute (صفة) only for the Companions (الصحب), not for the Family (آل). The commentator argues this because the comparison to stars applies only to the Companions.
This grammatical point sets the stage for the theological discussion.
2
Extract the two possible meanings of 'الهَدَى'
The word can mean either 'الاهْتِدَاء' (seeking guidance) or 'الهِدَايَة' (guidance itself). The commentator prefers the latter for this context.
The choice affects the interpretation of the poem.
3
Analyze the Sunni definition of 'الهِدَايَة'
According to Ahl al-Sunna, guidance is 'the indication of a path that leads to the goal, whether or not one actually arrives'. This emphasizes that guidance is the act of showing the way, not necessarily the arrival.
This definition is broad and includes both successful and unsuccessful guidance.
4
Examine the Mu'tazili condition and the scriptural refutation
The Mu'tazila add the condition that the person must actually arrive. The passage refutes this using the verse 'وَأَمَّا ثَمُودُ فَهَدَيْنَاهُمْ' (41:17), where Thamud were guided but did not reach the goal, yet their indication is called 'hidāya'.
This refutation shows that the term is used without requiring arrival.
5
Understand the objection and its rebuttal via the two 'individuals' of guidance
An objection is raised using 'إِنَّكَ لَا تَهْدِي مَنْ أَحْبَبْتَ' (28:56). The rebuttal: guidance has two individuals – one that leads to actual arrival and one that does not. The negated guidance in the verse is the one that ensures arrival, which only Allah can grant.
Thus, the verse does not contradict the Sunni definition; it only negates one type of guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (ذَوِي الهُدَى صِفَةٌ لِـ «الصَّحْبِ» فَقَط) ، وَكَذَا قَوْلُهُ : مَنْ شُبِّهوا ... إلخ»؛ لِأَنَّ التَّشْبِية لَيْسَ إِلَّا لِلصَّحْبِ، كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي، وَجَعْلُ الأَوَّل لِكُلِّ مِنَ الآلِ وَالصَّحْبِ، وَالثَّانِي لِلصَّحْبِ فَقَط لَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ البُعْدِ .
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ (الهُدَى»: الاهْتِدَاءُ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِهِ : الهِدَايَة (٢) :
- وَهِيَ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ : «الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَى طَرِيقٍ تُوصِلُ إِلَى المَقْصُودِ ؛ وُصِلَ بِالفِعْلِ أَوْ لَمْ يَصِلُّ». - وَعِنْدَ المُعْتَزِلَةِ : «الدَّلَالَةُ المَذْكُورَةُ لَكِنْ بِشَرْطِ أَنْ يَصِلَ بِالفِعْلِ»، ونُقِضَ بِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى : ﴿وَأَمَا ثَمُودُ فَهَدَيْنَهُمْ ﴾ [فصلت: ۱۷] الآيَةَ ، فَإِنَّهُمْ لَمْ يَصِلُوا بِالفِعْلِ، وَمَعَ ذَلِكَ سُمِّيَتْ دَلَالَتُهُمْ عَلَى طَرِيقٍ تُوصِلُ: «هِدَايَةٌ» .
وَأَوْرَدَ بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ : قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿إِنَّكَ لَا تَهْدِي مَنْ أَحْبَبْتَ) [القصص : ٥٦] ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يُرَادَ مِنْهُ: «الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَى طَرِيقٍ تُوصِلُ إِلَى المَقْصُودِ ؛ وُصِلَ بِالفِعْلِ أَوْ لَمْ يَصِلْ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ وُجِدَتْ مِنْهُ الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَى طَرِيقٍ تُوصِلُ، لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَصِلِ المَدْلُولَ بِالفِعْلِ.
وَأَنْتَ خَبِيرٌ بِأَنَّهُ مَدْفُوعٌ مِنْ أَصْلِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ مُرَادَ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ أَنَّ الهِدَايَةَ هِيَ الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَى طَرِيقٍ تُوَصِّلُ، وَلِهَذِهِ الدَّلَالَةِ فَرْدَانِ : المُوَصِّلَةُ بِالفِعْلِ وَغَيْرُهَا ، وَالمُرَادُ بِهَا فِي هَذِهِ الآيَةِ الفَرْدُ الأَوَّلُ؛ لأَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يَصِحُ نَفْيُهُ.
قوله : (وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِهِ الهِدَايَةُ) أي : للغير، ولا يتكرر حينئذ مع [ج/ ٩] قوله : مَنْ شُبِّهوا ... إلخ » ؛ لإفادة الثاني ما لم يُفده الأول.
قوله : (وَأَنْتَ خَبِيرٌ بِأَنَّهُ مَدْفُوعٌ ... إلخ) لا يخفى أنَّ إطلاق الكلي على فرده المخصوص تأويل، وللمعتزلة أن يقولوا بمثل هذا التَّأويل في الآية الأولى ؛ بأن يطلق الخاص على العام، ولذلك قال بعض
هَذَا ؛ وَفِي بَعْضِ التَّفَاسِيرِ تَفْسِيرُ الهِدَايَةِ فِي الآيَةِ المَذْكُورَةِ بِخَلْقِ الاِهْتِدَاءِ ، فَلْيُرَاجَعْ قَوْلُهُ : (مَنْ شُبِّهوا ... إلخ) أَشَارَ بِذَلِكَ إِلَى مَا رُوِيَ مِنْ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم سَأَلَ الرَّبَّ عَمَّا يَخْتَلِفُ فِيهِ أَصْحَابُهُ، فَقَالَ: «يَا مُحَمَّدُ! أَصْحَابُكَ عِنْدِي كَالنُّجُومِ فِي السَّمَاءِ، بَعْضُهَا أَضْوَءُ مِنْ بَعْضٍ فَمَنْ أَخَذَ بِشَيْءٍ مِمَّا اخْتَلَفُوا فِيهِ فَهُوَ عَلَى هَدْيِ عِنْدِي » (٣) ، وَإِلَى مَا رُوِيَ أَيْضاً مِنْ أَنَّهُ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «أَصْحَابِي كَالنُّجُومِ، بِأَيْهِمُ اقْتَدَيْتُمُ اهْتَدَيْتُمْ ) .
His statement: (ذَوِي الهُدَى is an attribute only for 'الصَّحْبِ'), and likewise his statement: 'مَنْ شُبِّهوا ... etc.' because the comparison is only for the Companions, as will be known from what follows. Making the first for both the Family and the Companions, and the second only for the Companions, the awkwardness in it is not hidden. And what is meant by 'الهَدَى' is 'الاهْتِدَاء' (seeking guidance), and it is possible that what is meant by it is 'الهِدَايَة' (guidance). And it, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is 'the indication of a path that leads to the goal, whether or not one actually arrives (bi-l-fi'l)'. And according to the Mu'tazila: 'the aforementioned indication but with the condition that one actually arrives'. And it is refuted by His statement, Exalted: 'And as for Thamud, We guided them' [Fussilat:17] — the verse — for they did not arrive in actuality, yet their indication of a path that leads is called 'هداية'. And some objected to the first with His statement, Exalted: 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love' [al-Qasas:56], for it is not correct that what is meant by it is 'the indication of a path that leads to the goal whether or not one actually arrives'; because indication of a path that leads existed from him (the Prophet), but he did not make the indicated one reach the goal in actuality. And you are well aware that it is repelled from its root; because what Ahl al-Sunna mean is that guidance is the indication of a path that leads, and this indication has two individuals: that which leads to actual arrival and other than it. And what is meant by it in this verse is the first individual, because it is that which can be negated. His statement: (And it is possible that what is meant by it is guidance) i.e., for others, and then it does not repeat with his statement 'مَنْ شُبِّهوا... etc.' because the second conveys what the first did not. His statement: (And you are well aware that it is repelled... etc.) It is not hidden that applying the universal to its particular individual is an interpretation (ta'wil), and the Mu'tazila can say a similar interpretation in the first verse — that is, applying the particular to the universal. Therefore, some said: This; and in some commentaries, the interpretation of guidance in the mentioned verse is 'creating the state of seeking guidance'. So let it be referred to. His statement: (مَنْ شُبِّهوا... etc.) pointed by that to what is narrated: that the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, asked the Lord about what his Companions would differ in, and He said: 'O Muhammad! Your Companions with Me are like stars in the sky, some brighter than others. Whoever takes anything from what they differed in is upon a guidance from Me.' And also to what is narrated: that he, peace be upon him, said: 'My Companions are like stars; whichever of them you follow, you will be guided.'
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of the poem: 'وَآلِهِ وَصَحْبِهِ ذَوِي الهُدَى مَنْ شُبِّهُوا بِأَنْجُمِ فِي الاهْتِدَا'. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by clarifying the grammatical relationship: the phrase 'ذَوِي الهُدَى' (possessors of guidance) is an attribute solely for 'الصَّحْبِ' (the Companions), not for the Family (آل). He argues that the comparison to stars applies only to the Companions, as will be evident later. He then discusses the meaning of 'الهَدَى': it can mean 'الاهْتِدَاء' (the act of seeking guidance) or 'الهِدَايَة' (guidance itself). This leads to a theological discussion on the definition of 'هداية' (guidance). According to Ahl al-Sunna, guidance is 'the indication of a path that leads to the goal, whether or not the person actually arrives at that goal'. The Mu'tazila, however, condition that the person must actually arrive. The commentator then provides a refutation (naqd) of the Mu'tazila position using the verse 'وَأَمَّا ثَمُودُ فَهَدَيْنَاهُمْ' (And as for Thamud, We guided them), where the people of Thamud were guided (by being shown the path) but did not actually follow it. Despite not arriving, their being shown the path is called 'هداية'. Then, an objection (irad) is raised against the Ahl al-Sunna definition using the verse 'إِنَّكَ لَا تَهْدِي مَنْ أَحْبَبْتَ' (Indeed, you do not guide whom you love). The objection argues that if guidance is simply showing the path, then the Prophet did show the path to his uncle Abu Talib, yet the verse negates guidance from him. The commentator, al-Bajuri, rebuts this by explaining that Ahl al-Sunna consider guidance to have two 'individuals' (afrad): guidance that results in actual arrival and guidance that does not. The negated guidance in the verse is the one that results in actual arrival, which only Allah can grant. Thus, the verse does not contradict their definition. Al-Anbabi, another commentator, adds that the universal application of guidance to its particular individual is a form of interpretation (ta'wil), and the Mu'tazila could similarly interpret the first verse. He notes that some scholars hold that the term 'guidance' is linguistically used for both meanings, so there is no real dispute. Finally, the passage connects the poetic line to a hadith: the Prophet asked Allah about what his Companions would differ in, and Allah replied that the Companions are like stars, some brighter than others, and whoever follows any of them is upon guidance. This hadith underscores the reliability of the Companions and justifies following their differing opinions. The passage thus clarifies the meaning of guidance in the poem and defends the Sunni position, while also emphasizing the elevated status of the Companions.

Key Terms
الهَدَى
الهِدَايَة
الدَّلَالَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The act of seeking guidance.
B) The indication of a path that leads to the goal, whether or not one actually arrives.

D) Creating the state of seeking guidance in the heart.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni definition of guidance (al-hidāya) is broad: it is the indication of the path, independent of whether the person reaches the goal; this resolves apparent scriptural contradictions.

Logic as the Grammar of the Mind
Author defines logic as a tool for the mind analogous to grammar for language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the analogy between logic and grammar and state the function of logic.

Core Idea
Logic safeguards the mind from error, just as grammar safeguards speech.

The poet opens with a memorable analogy: logic relates to the mind as grammar relates to the tongue. This comparison is not decorative—it defines the very purpose of logic. Grammar prescribes rules to prevent mistakes in speech, and logic does the same for reasoning. Understanding this analogy is the first step to grasping the entire science of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sciences compared
Read the line: logic (al-manṭiq) and grammar (al-naḥw). These are two distinct sciences.
2
Determine the subject each science governs
Logic governs the mind (al-janān, also called al-'aql), while grammar governs the tongue (al-lisān).
The mind here means the rational soul, the faculty of reasoning.
3
State the purpose of each
Grammar protects speech from error; logic protects thought from error. Both are prescriptive: they give rules to achieve correctness.
4
Apply the analogy to a concrete case
Just as a grammarian corrects a grammatical mistake, a logician corrects a reasoning mistake. For example, saying 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, nabidh is an intoxicant, therefore nabidh is forbidden' is valid because of the logical form.
The tool of logic ensures the argument is valid independent of content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٩) وَبَعْدُ: فَالمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ كَالنَّحْوِلِلِّسَانِ

قَوْلُهُ : (وَبَعْدُ) أَصْلُ هَذِهِ الكَلِمَةِ : «أَمَّا بَعْدُ، وَالأَصْلُ الأَصِيلِ : «مَهْمَا يَكُنْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ بَعْدُ»، فَحُذِفَ كُلٌّ مِنْ مَهْمَا) وَ(يَكُنْ) وَمِنْ شَيْءٍ ; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّ التَّرْكِيبَ حَقُّهُ أَنْ يَكُونَ هَكَذَا، وَلَمْ يُوْتَ
(9) And after: logic is for the mind as grammar is for the tongue.

His statement: 'And after' - the origin of this word is 'amma ba'du', and the original root is 'mahma yakun min shay'in ba'du', so each of 'mahma', 'yakun', and 'min shay'in' is deleted; meaning that the construction ought to be like this, and it was not brought...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage contains the core definition of logic from the poem. The poet states that logic (al-manṭiq) relates to the mind (al-janān) as grammar (al-naḥw) relates to the tongue (al-lisān). This is a foundational analogy: grammar prescribes rules to prevent error in speech, and logic does the same for the intellect. The commentary then explains the etymology of the word 'ba'du' in the transitional phrase 'wa ba'du', showing its origin from 'amma ba'du' and the deletion of certain elements. This grammatical explanation sets the stage for introducing the science of logic.

Key Takeaway
Logic is a normative science for the intellect, analogous to grammar's role for language.

The Definition of Mantiq and the Ten Introductory Principles
Author defines mantiq and lists the ten introductory principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of mantiq and explain the function of the ten introductory principles in any Islamic science.

Core Idea
Logic's definition mirrors its benefit: converting knowns into unknown truths.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary establishes the definition of mantiq as a science of conceptual and assertoric knowns. But the author does so by first resolving a grammatical objection and then linking the definition to a broader framework: the ten principles that every systematic science must have. This double move—clarifying the language and situating the definition within a metatheory—reveals how seriously medieval scholars took the foundations of their disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Locate the formal definition of mantiq
Find the Arabic sentence that begins with 'fahaddhu hadha al-fann'. This is the core technical definition: 'ilm yubhathu fihi 'an al-ma'lumat al-tasawwuriyya wa al-tasdiqiyya.
The definition appears after the poem quoting the ten principles.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'science' ('ilm). The differentia is 'investigates conceptual and assertoric known things'. Confirm against the poem's reference to 'al-ma'lumat al-tasawwuriyya wa al-tasdiqiyya'.
Conceptual knowns (tasawwur) are simple ideas; assertoric knowns (tasdiq) are judgments.
3
Identify the ten principles mentioned in the poem
The poem lists ten items: definition, subject, benefit, superiority, relation, founder, name, derivation, ruling of the legislator, and issues. Note that the definition under discussion is one of these principles.
These principles are used to frame any science in the post-classical tradition.
4
Understand the grammatical objection and its resolution
The author addresses why the apodosis must be consequent on the condition. This shows that even grammatical precision safeguards the logical soundness of the definition.
The objection concerns the use of 'fa' in the conditional construction.
5
Connect the definition to the benefit of logic
The benefit (thamara) is the ability to know the unknown from the known. The definition and benefit are linked: the science of logic provides the rules for correct inference.
The benefit is part of the ten principles, showing the holistic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (فَالمَنْطِقُ ... إلخ) أَيْ : «فَأَقُولُ : المَنْطِقُ ... إلخ ) . فَانْدَفَعَ مَا يَرِدُ مِنْ : أَنَّهُ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَضْمُونُ الجَزَاءِ مُرَتَّباً عَلَى فِعْل الشَّرْطِ . وَوَجْهُ الاِنْدِفَاعِ : أَنَّ مَضْمُونَ الجَزَاءِ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ الإِخْبَارُ بِالكَوْنِ المَذْكُورِ، لَا نَفْسُهُ، وَلَا شَكٍّ أَنَّهُ مُتَرَتِّبٌ عَلَى فِعْلِ الشَّرْطِ . نَعَمْ؛ يَرِدُ حِينَئِذٍ : أَنَّهُمْ نَصُّوا عَلَى أَنَّهُ يَجِبُ حَذْفَ الفَاءِ إِذَا كَانَ المَحْذُوفُ قَوْلاً . وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ هَذَا لَيْسَ مُتَّفَقاً عَلَيْهِ، بَلْ طَرِيقَةٌ لِبَعْضِهِمْ، فَيَكُونُ المُصَنِّفُ قَدْ جَرَى عَلَى الطَّرِيقَةِ الأُخْرَى القَائِلَةِ بِعَدَمِ وُجُوبِ حَذْفِ الفَاءِ حِينَئِذٍ، كَمَا نَقَلَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ عَنْ هَمْعِ الهَوَامِعِ لِلسُّيُوطِيِّ . وَأَشَارَ المُصَنِّفُ بِهَذَا إِلَى ثَمَرَةِ هَذَا الفَنِّ الَّتِي هِيَ أَحَدُ المَبَادِئ العَشَرَةِ المَنْظُومَةِ فِي قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ : [من الرجز] إِنَّ مَبَادِي كُلِّ فَنْ عَشَرَهُ الحَدُّ والمَوْضُوعُ ثُمَّ الثَّمَرَهُ وَفَضْلُهُ وَنِسْبَةٌ وَالوَاضِعْ وَالاسْمُ الاسْتِمْدَادُ حُكْمُ الشَّارِعُ مَسَائِلٌ وَالبَعْضُ بِالبَعْضِ اكْتَفَى وَمَنْ دَرَى الجَمِيعَ حَازَ الشَّرَفا - فَحَدُّ هَذَا الفَنِّ : عِلْمٌ يُبْحَثُ فِيهِ عَنِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ ;
His saying: 'So logic... etc.' meaning: 'So I say: logic... etc.' Thus what is objected (that the content of the apodosis must be consequent upon the act of the condition) is repelled. The aspect of repelling is that the content of the apodosis in reality is the informing of the mentioned state, not the state itself, and there is no doubt that it is consequent upon the act of the condition. Yes, then an objection arises: they stated that the fa' must be omitted when the omitted (verb) is 'qawl' (saying). And it is answered that this is not agreed upon; rather, it is a method of some of them. So the author follows the other method that holds there is no obligation to omit the fa' in that case, as some have transmitted from 'Ham' al-Hawami' of al-Suyuti. And the author indicated by this the benefit of this art, which is one of the ten principles versified in the saying of some: 'Indeed the principles of every art are ten: the definition, the subject matter, then the benefit, its superiority and relation, the founder, the name, the derivation, the ruling of the legislator, and the issues. Some suffice with some, and whoever knows all has attained honor.' So the definition of this art is: a science in which is discussed the conceptual and assertoric known things.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and introductory commentary on the matn line 'فالمنطق للجنات نسبة كالنحو للسان'. The author first clarifies that the phrase 'wa ba'd' implies a conditional sentence where the apodosis is 'fa-al-mantiq...' and addresses an objection about the need for the apodosis to be consequent upon the condition. He explains that the real apodosis is the act of informing, not the state described, so the objection is resolved. Then he discusses a secondary objection about omitting the 'fa' when the omitted verb is 'qawl', showing the author follows a lenient view. Then he notes that this whole discussion points to the benefit (thamara) of the science of logic, which is one of the ten introductory principles of any science. A poem listing these ten principles is quoted: definition, subject matter, benefit, superiority, relation, founder, name, derivation, ruling, and issues. Finally, the technical definition of logic is given: it is a science in which one investigates conceptual and assertoric known things. This definition captures the subject matter: the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) insofar as they lead to knowledge of the unknown.

Key Terms

ثمرة الفن
المبادئ العشرة

Defining Logic and Its Two Perspectives
Author defines logic as a science investigating conceptual and assentional knowns in two respects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two perspectives from which logic investigates conceptual and assentional knowns and explain what each perspective entails.

Core Idea
Logic studies both the tools that lead to unknowns and the foundations of those tools.

This lesson unpacks the formal definition of logic given by the author. The definition is not just a label; it tells us precisely what logic does and what its subject matter is. Al-Anbabi's commentary reveals the depth behind the words, showing that logic involves two layers of investigation: the actual tools (definitions and propositions) that bring us from known to unknown, and the prior analysis of the knowns themselves (e.g., is it universal or particular, essential or accidental). Understanding this dual perspective is key to grasping how logic serves as an instrument for all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
The definiendum is 'this art' (logic). The definiens is 'a science in which is investigated the conceptual and assentional knowns'. Locate these in the Arabic text.
2
Break down the two perspectives in the definiens
The definiens includes two perspectives: (1) 'from the perspective that they lead to an unknown thing' and (2) 'from the perspective of what that depends on'. Find the Arabic phrases for these.
The 'or' (أَوْ ) indicates two distinct angles.
3
Understand the first perspective: knowns as means to unknowns
Al-Anbabi explains that the rules for conceptual knowns leading to conceptual unknowns are the rules of definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). Similarly, assentional knowns lead to assentional unknowns through syllogisms (though not explicit here).
The tools for conceptual unknowns are definitions; for assentional unknowns, the tool is the syllogism.
4
Understand the second perspective: prior analysis of knowns
The second perspective investigates the knowns themselves: for a conceptual known, we ask: universal or particular? essential or accidental? genus or species? For an assentional known, we analyze the subject and predicate, and whether the proposition is sound, etc.
This analysis is prior to constructing definitions or arguments.
5
See how the two perspectives relate
The second perspective provides the raw materials (e.g., identifying a conceptual known as a genus) for the first perspective (building a definition that uses that genus). Both are essential to logic.
Al-Anbabi says the second investigation is 'prior' to the first in the order of learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَحَدُّ هَذَا الفَنِّ : عِلْمٌ يُبْحَثُ فِيهِ عَنِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ ؛ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٌّ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِيِّ ، أَوْ مِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ .
الأنبابي
قوله : (مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ ... إلخ) والقواعد الباحثة عن المعلومات التَّصوريَّة مِن حيث إنَّها توصل إلى مجهول تصوُّري هي القواعد المتعلقة بالحدود والرُّسوم، فإنَّ الحدود والرُّسوم توصل للمجهولات التصوريَّة بلا واسطة وعُقِدَ لذلك باب المعرفات.
||
وكمدلول قوله أيضاً : المعلومُ التَّصوريُّ: يُبحث عنه المعرِّف أهو كلّيّ أو جزئي؟ فإن كان كلِّيًّا أخذه وبعد ذلك أهو ذاتي أو عرضيّ؟ فإن كان ذاتيًا أخذه أيضاً وبعد ذلك أهو جنسٌ أو فصل؟ فإن كان جنساً أخذه وقدَّمه في الذكر كما تقدَّم، والمعلوم التصديقي : يبحث المعرّف عن الموضوع والمحمول أوّلاً هل الموضوع شخصي أو كلي؟ والمحمول ثابت له أو لا؟ فإن كان ثابتاً له يبحث في هذا التركيب أهو قضيَّةٌ أو عكس قضية ؛ صحيح أو فاسد أو نقيض قضية كذلك؟ تأمل . وكتب ثانياً : قوله : (عِلْمٌ يُبْحَثُ فِيهِ ... إلخ) أي : نسب كلِّيَّة يقع الإخبار فيها عن المعلومات التصورية بأنَّها تركب على الهيئة المخصوصة لأجل التوصل إلى الأمر المجهول، وبأنَّها [...] عليها بأنَّها كلِّيَّة أو جزئيَّةٌ ؛ ذاتيَّةٌ أو عرضيَّة . .. إلخ، وهذا البحث الثاني سابق [على] المعرّف على الأوَّل، وكذا المعلومات التصديقيَّة بأنَّها تركَّب على الهيئة المخصوصة لتوصل ... إلخ، وبأنَّها قضيَّةٌ أو عكس قضية ... إلخ، وهذا البحث أيضاً سابق على البحث الأول.
So the definition of this art: a science in which is investigated the conceptual and assentional knowns, from the perspective that they lead to an unknown thing, conceptual or assentional, or from the perspective of what that depends on. Al-Anbabi: His saying (from the perspective that they lead... etc.) The rules investigating the conceptual knowns from the perspective that they lead to an unknown conceptual thing are the rules related to definitions and descriptions, for definitions and descriptions lead to conceptual unknowns without intermediary, and for that a chapter on definers has been established. And similarly according to his statement also: The conceptual known: the definer investigates whether it is universal or particular; if it is universal, he takes it, and then whether it is essential or accidental; if essential, he takes it, and then whether it is genus or differentia; if genus, he takes it and presents it in mention as previously. And the assentional known: the definer investigates the subject and predicate first whether the subject is individual or universal, and whether the predicate is affirmed for it or not; if affirmed for it, he investigates in this composition whether it is a proposition or the converse of a proposition, sound or unsound, or the contradictory of a proposition likewise. Reflect. And he wrote secondly: His saying (a science in which is investigated... etc.) means: universal relations in which information occurs about the conceptual knowns that they are composed in the specific form for the sake of arriving at the unknown thing, and that they [؟] as universal or particular, essential or accidental... etc., and this second investigation is prior to the definer over the first, and similarly the assentional knowns that they are composed in the specific form to lead... etc., and that they are a proposition or converse of a proposition... etc., and this investigation is also prior to the first investigation.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage provides the formal definition (hadd) of the art of logic. The author defines it as 'a science in which is investigated the conceptual and assentional knowns' with two perspectives: (1) from the perspective that these knowns lead to an unknown thing (conceptual or assentional), and (2) from the perspective of what that leading depends on. Al-Anbabi's commentary explains the first perspective: the rules concerning conceptual knowns that lead to unknown concepts are the rules of definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). He then elaborates how a definer investigates conceptual knowns: first determining if it is universal/particular, then essential/accidental, then genus/species. For assentional knowns, the definer investigates the subject/predicate relation, and whether the proposition is sound or unsound, etc. Al-Anbabi also notes a second type of investigation: the study of the universal relations that are composed in a specific form to lead to the unknown, and the classification of knowns as universal/particular, essential/accidental. This second investigation is actually prior to the first in the order of learning. Thus, logic involves both the study of how knowns lead to unknowns (the tools of definition and syllogism) and the study of the knowns themselves as universal/particular, essential/accidental, which is the foundation for constructing definitions and arguments.

Key Terms
المَعْلُومَات التَّصَوُّرِيَّة
المَعْلُومَات التَّصْدِيقِيَّة
الحدود والرُّسوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Four: universal, particular, essential, accidental

Key Takeaway
Logic is defined by two investigative angles: how knowns lead to unknowns and the prior analysis of the knowns themselves—both essential for rigorous inquiry in Islamic sciences.

Resolving Ambiguity in Logical Definitions: The Case of the Demonstrative Pronoun
Al-Shirshimi critiques the interpretation of the two aspects and resolves objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical ambiguity in a definition's pronoun referent and apply the method of implied annexation to resolve it, following al-Shirshimi's analysis.

Core Idea
The correct referent of 'that' in logic's definition resolves the tension between the two perspectives.

In defining logic, scholars debated whether the second perspective 'from the perspective of what that depends on' referred directly to the process of leading or to something else. Al-Shirshimi uncovers two logical objections to the common interpretation and proposes a refined reading using an implied annexation. This lesson walks you through his analytical method, which is a model for resolving ambiguities in technical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Locate the phrase 'من حيث ما يتوقف عليه ذلك' in the definition. Note that the demonstrative pronoun 'ذلك' ('that') can refer to different antecedents, leading to different understandings of what the second perspective investigates.
The phrase appears in the standard definition of logic: 'علم يعرف به من حيث التوصل... أو من حيث ما يتوقف عليه ذلك'
2
Analyze the two objections
If 'ذلك' refers directly to 'التوصّل' (leading), two objections arise: (1) Why is the investigation of composition not included, since leading also depends on it? (2) How can leading depend on each single proposition without intermediary, when it actually depends on the combination of two premises? Understand why these are problems.
The first objection challenges the completeness of 'what it depends on', the second challenges the directness of dependence.
3
Consider an alternative interpretation
Some suggest 'ذلك' refers to 'المعلومات' (the knowns). Evaluate why this is less satisfactory: it repels the first objection but introduces other issues, as al-Shirshimi notes.
'معلومات' is another possible referent from the context.
4
Apply the implied annexation solution
Al-Shirshimi's preferred solution: keep 'ذلك' referring to 'التوصّل', but add an implied noun 'سبب' (cause). The phrase becomes 'from the perspective of what the cause of that leading depends on'. The cause is the composition in the specific form. Verify that this resolves both objections: composition is now directly investigated, and dependence on each proposition is mediated by the composition.
This preserves the original wording while adding precision.
5
Validate the refinement
Check that no new problems arise: the refined interpretation aligns with the logical structure of inference (leading depends on composition, which depends on premises) and maintains the definition's coherence. Reflect on why this is 'أحسن' (best).
This step involves logical consistency checking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرشيمي : وقوله : (مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ ، .. أَوْ مِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ أي من أجل البحث عن سبب التوصل مِنَ السَّبب [..]، ومن حيث البحث عما يتوقف عليه ذلك التَّوصُّل ؛ بناءً على أنَّ اسم الإشارة راجع إلى التَّوصُّل المفهوم مِنَ الفعل كما هو الظاهر . لكن يرد عليه أمران: الأوَّل : كيف جعلتم البحث عن الكلية ... إلخ وعن القضية بحثاً عما يتوقف عليه التّوصل، ولم تجعلوا البحث التركيب بحثاً عما يتوقف عليه ذلك، مع أنه متوقف على التركيب أيضاً؟ والثَّاني : أنَّ التَّوصُّل متوقف على مجموع القضيَّتين، فكيف تجعلوا التَّوقُّف على كلِّ قضيَّةٍ بلا واسطة، فهذا يبعد عن هذا الفهم . ويحتمل على بعد: أنَّ اسم الإشارة راجع إلى المعلومات كما قاله بعضهم، وعلى هذا يرد الإيراد الأول بالثاني . والأحسن من هذا كله : إرجاعه إلى التَّوصُّل كما قلنا ، لكن على تقدير مضاف ؛ أي: ومن حيث ما يتوقف عليه سبب ذلك التوصل»، وذلك السَّبب هو التّركيب على الهيئة المخصوصة ؛ لأنه سبب قريب إليه . وإنَّما كان هذا أحسن؛ لأنَّه لا يرد عليه الإيراد الأول وهو ظاهر، ولا الثَّاني؛ لأنَّ توقفه على كلِّ قضيَّةٍ بلا واسطة؛ تأمل، والله تعالى الموفق . (۲) الشرشيمي : قوله : (أَوْ مِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ أي : مِن حيث ما يتوقف عليه التَّوصُّل ؛ هذا هو المتعين من مدلول اسم الإشارة من أنه راجع إلى التوصل .
Al-Shirshimi: And his saying (from the perspective that they lead... or from the perspective of what that depends on) means: for the sake of investigating the cause of leading from the cause..., and from the perspective of investigating what that leading depends on, based on the fact that the demonstrative pronoun refers to the leading understood from the verb, as is apparent. But two objections occur to him: First: How did you make the investigation of universality... etc. and of the proposition an investigation into what the leading depends on, and you did not make the investigation of composition an investigation into what it depends on, while it also depends on composition? Second: That the leading depends on the totality of the two propositions, so how do you make the dependence on each proposition without intermediary? This is far from this understanding. And it is probable, with distance, that the demonstrative pronoun refers to the knowns as some said, and on this the first objection is repelled by the second. And the best among all this is to refer it to the leading as we said, but with an implied annexed phrase: i.e., 'from the perspective of what the cause of that leading depends on', and that cause is the composition in the specific form because it is the proximate cause. And it was better because the first objection does not occur to it, which is apparent, and nor the second, because its dependence on each proposition without intermediary; reflect. And God Almighty is the giver of success. (2) Al-Shirshimi: His saying (or from the perspective of what that depends on) means: from the perspective of what the leading depends on; this is the determined meaning of the demonstrative pronoun that it refers to the leading.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by al-Shirshimi on the interpretation of the second perspective in the definition of logic: 'or from the perspective of what that depends on'. He argues that the demonstrative 'that' refers to the act of leading (al-tawassul) itself, not the knowns. He then raises two objections to this interpretation: (1) If the perspective is about what the leading depends on, then why do commentators classify the investigation of universality, essentiality, etc. (which are properties of the knowns) as part of 'what the leading depends on'? And why is the investigation of composition (the specific form of the proposition) not included? (2) The leading depends on the totality of two propositions (the premises), so how can we say it depends on each single proposition directly? Al-Shirshimi then considers an alternative: referring the demonstrative to the knowns, but that also has problems. His preferred solution is to keep referring to the leading but with an implied annexation: the perspective is about what the cause of the leading depends on, and that cause is the composition in the specific form. This avoids both objections because the composition is the proximate cause of the leading, and its dependence on each premise is through the composition. This refinement shows the careful exegetical work in making the definition logically precise.

Key Terms
التَّوصُّل
اسم الإشارة
التركيب على الهيئة المخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Shirshimi, what is the best way to interpret the demonstrative pronoun 'ذلك' in the definition of logic?
A) It refers directly to the knowns (المعلومات).
B) It refers directly to the leading (التوصّل).
C) It refers to the leading with an implied annexation 'cause of leading'.
D) It refers to composition itself.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shirshimi's refinement shows that precision in referent identification is essential for a logically sound definition, and the method of implied annexation can resolve apparent contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In the definition of hadith scrutiny ('علم الجرح والتعديل'), a phrase says 'من حيث ما يتوقف عليه ذلك' (from the perspective of what that depends on). 5 min
Logic's Dual Focus: Definitions and the Path to Knowledge
Author defines logic and illustrates its two investigatory aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two aspects of logic's investigation—as means to unknown knowledge and as the prerequisites for those means—and identify examples of each from Islamic definitions.

Core Idea
Logic is the grammar of the mind, guiding both definitions and arguments.

This passage introduces logic's role as a mental instrument, akin to grammar for speech. It clarifies that logic studies known concepts and judgments from two angles: as tools that lead to new knowledge, and as the foundational attributes that make those tools effective. This lesson unpacks that distinction with the concrete example of genus and differentia in defining 'human'.

Guided Path
1
Identify logic's role
Note the analogy: logic relates to the mind as grammar relates to the tongue. Both are regulatory instruments.
This analogy emphasises that logic is not a science of content but a tool for correct thinking.
2
Extract the two aspects
The text says logic investigates known things from two aspects: (1) as means that lead to an unknown, and (2) as what that leading depends upon.
The first aspect deals with definitions and syllogisms; the second with the properties of the knowns themselves.
3
Analyse the example
The conceptual knowns 'animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia) when composed in the specific manner yield the unknown concept 'human'.
This illustrates the first aspect: using knowns to arrive at an unknown conceptual matter.
4
Recognise the second aspect
After identifying genus and differentia, logic examines whether they are universal or particular, essential or accidental, etc.
These attributes are prerequisites for ensuring the definition is sound.

وَبَعْدُ: فَالْمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِ لِلِّسَانِ. مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٍّ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِيٍّ، أَوْ مِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ. مِثَالُ البَحْثِ عَنِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٍّ : البَحْثُ عَنِ الجِنْسِ وَالفَصْلِ كَـ: «الحَيَوَانِ وَالنَّاطِقِ» وَهُمَا مَعْلُومَانِ تَصَوُّرِيَّانِ، بِأَنَّهُمَا إِذَا رُكِّبَا عَلَى الوَجْهِ المَخْصُوصِ وُصِّلَ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٍّ كَـ: الإِنْسَانِ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ : البَحْثُ عَمَّا ذُكِرَ بِأَنَّهُ كُلِّيٌّ أَوْ جُزْئِيٌّ، ذَاتِيٌّ أَوْ عَرَضِيٌّ، جِنْسٌ أَوْ فَصْلٌ.
And afterward: Logic's relation to the mind is like grammar's relation to the tongue. From the aspect that they (conceptual and veridical known things) lead to an unknown conceptual or veridical matter, or from the aspect of what that (leading) depends upon. An example of investigating conceptual known things from the aspect that they lead to an unknown conceptual matter: investigating genus and species, such as 'animal' and 'rational', which are two conceptual known things, that when they are composed in the specific manner, their total leads to an unknown conceptual matter like 'human'; and from the aspect of what that depends upon: investigating what was mentioned (genus and species) as to whether it is universal or particular, essential or accidental, genus or species.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of logic and clarifies its two fundamental investigatory aspects. It begins by stating that logic's relation to the mind is analogous to grammar's relation to the tongue—just as grammar regulates speech, logic regulates thought. Then it explains that logic studies known things (conceptual and veridical) from two angles: (1) as means that lead to an unknown—conceptual unknown via definitions and descriptions, veridical unknown via syllogisms and arguments; (2) as what that leading depends upon, i.e., the attributes of the known things themselves that must be examined before they can be used as means. The text provides a concrete example: investigating the conceptual knowns 'animal' and 'rational' to see that when combined in the specific manner (genus and differentia), they yield the unknown concept 'human'. Then it adds that from the second aspect, one investigates whether these knowns are universal or particular, essential or accidental, genus or species—prerequisites for constructing a proper definition.

Bajuri on Conceptual and Assertoric Knowledge in Logic
Bajuri defines the scope of logic by giving examples of conceptual and assertoric investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conceptual and assertoric knowledge in logic, and identify the components of each type of investigation as presented by Bajuri.

Core Idea
Logic governs both definition (concept composition) and argument (assertion proof).

In the opening of his commentary, Bajuri explains the subject matter of logic by analogy: logic is to the heart as grammar is to the tongue. He then unpacks this by giving concrete examples for the two main types of logical investigation: conceptual (tasawwur) and assertoric (tasdiq). Understanding this distinction is foundational for the entire discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Bajuri says logic for the heart is like grammar for the tongue. What does this suggest about its function?
The heart here represents the intellect; logic regulates thinking just as grammar regulates speech.
2
Examine the conceptual example
He uses the definition of 'human' from 'animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia). Note that both are known concepts that combine to produce an unknown concept.
3
Examine the assertoric example
He uses the syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' Two known premises yield an unknown conclusion.
4
Note the dependencies
For conceptual knowledge, logic also investigates the properties of the components (universal/particular, essential/accidental); for assertoric knowledge, it investigates the premises themselves (whether they are propositions, converses, contradictories) and their subject-predicate structure.

Source Text

مقدمة الناظم
(۹) وَبَعْدُ: فَالمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِلِلِّسَانِ
الباجوري
مِثَالُ البَحْثِ عَنِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٍّ : البَحْثُ عَنِ الجِنْسِ وَالفَصْلِ كَـ: «الحَيَوَانِ وَالنَّاطِقِ وَهُمَا مَعْلُومَانِ تَصَوُّرِيَّانِ، بِأَنَّهُمَا إِذَا رُكْبَا عَلَى الوَجْهِ المَخْصُوصِ وُصَّلَ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٍّ كَـ : الإِنْسَانِ»، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ : البَحْثُ عَمَّا ذُكِرَ بِأَنَّهُ كُلِّيّ أَوْ جُزْنِيٌّ ، ذَاتِي أَوْ عَرَضِيٌّ، جِنْسٌ أَوْ فَصْلٌ.
وَمِثَالُ البَحْثِ عَنِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصْدِيقِيٍّ : البَحْثُ عَنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيِ القِيَاسِ كَقَوْلِنَا : العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرٌ ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ وَهُمَا مَعْلُومَانِ تَصْدِيقِيَّانِ، بِأَنَّهُمَا إِذَا رُكِّبَا عَلَى الوَجْهِ المَخْصُوصِ وَصَّلَ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصْدِيقِيٍّ كَقَوْلِنَا : العَالَمُ حَادِثٌ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ تَوَقُّفاً قَرِيباً لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ وَاسِطَةٍ: البَحْثُ عَنْ كُلٍّ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيِ القِيَاسِ بِأَنَّهُ قَضِيَّةٌ أَوْ عَكْسُ قَضِيَّةٍ، أَوْ نَقِيضُ قَضِيَّةٍ ، أَوْ تَوَقُّفاً بَعِيداً لِكَوْنِهِ بِوَاسِطَةِ تَوَقُّفِهِ عَلَى القَضِيَّةِ: البَحْثُ عَمَّا ذُكِرَ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَوْضُوعُ وَالمَحْمُولُ.
Introduction of the author: (9) And after: Logic for the heart is like grammar for the tongue. Al-Bajuri: An example of investigating conceptual knowledge in terms of its leading to an unknown conceptual matter: investigation of genus and species, like 'animal' and 'rational' – both are known conceptual premises – that when they are composed in the specific manner their combined effect leads to an unknown conceptual thing, like 'human'. And in terms of what that depends on: investigation of what was mentioned as to whether it is universal or particular, essential or accidental, genus or species. And an example of investigating assertoric knowledge in terms of its leading to an unknown assertoric matter: investigation of the two premises of the syllogism, like our saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated' – both are known assertoric premises – that when they are composed in the specific manner their combined effect leads to an unknown assertoric thing, like our saying: 'The world is originated.' And in terms of what that depends on, a close dependence because it is without intermediary: investigation of each of the two premises of the syllogism as to whether it is a proposition or the converse of a proposition, or the contradictory of a proposition; or a distant dependence because it is with an intermediary of its dependence on the proposition: investigation of what was mentioned in terms of subject and predicate.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is definitional: Bajuri, commenting on the opening verse of the matn, explains the subject matter of logic by providing concrete examples. The verse likens logic to the heart's grammar, paralleling grammar's role for the tongue. Bajuri then unpacks this by distinguishing two types of investigation: conceptual (tasawwuri) and assertoric (tasdiqi). For conceptual knowledge, he gives the example of defining 'human' by composing the known concepts 'animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia). The investigation here is into the parts of the definition and their properties (universal/particular, essential/accidental). For assertoric knowledge, he gives the example of a syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' The investigation here is into the premises of the syllogism, whether they are simple propositions, converses, or contradictories, and then into their subjects and predicates. Thus, logic covers both the route from known concepts to unknown concepts (via definition) and from known assertions to unknown assertions (via syllogism). The passage uses Islamic examples: 'human' from 'animal' and 'rational' (definition), and 'world is originated' from premises about change (theological argument).

Logic has two primary objects: conceptual unknowns (addressed by definitions) and assertoric unknowns (addressed by syllogisms).

Logic's Role: Regulating the Mind Like Grammar Regulates Speech
Definition of logic via analogy to grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the analogy between logic and grammar and identify the domain of logic as the regulation of internal thought.

Core Idea
Logic is to the mind what grammar is to the tongue—an internal regulator of thought.

The author opens with a poetic verse that encapsulates the entire purpose of logic. He compares logic to grammar, but with a crucial shift: grammar governs external speech, while logic governs the internal faculty of thought (al-jinan). This analogy sets the stage for understanding logic as an instrumental science that ensures correct reasoning, just as grammar ensures correct speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of the analogy
What two things are being compared? (Logic for jinan, grammar for lisan). Locate the verse: 'logic's relation to the mind is like grammar's relation to the tongue.'
2
Understand the function of grammar
Grammar regulates speech. By analogy, what does logic regulate? Think about how grammar corrects errors in speech; logic corrects errors in reasoning.
3
Recognize the domain of logic
Internal thought (jinan) vs external speech (lisan). Why is this distinction important? Logic deals with the mental processes that precede and inform speech.
4
Apply the analogy
How would you explain logic to someone using this analogy? Provide a concrete example from Islamic sciences (e.g., how a faqih uses logic to ensure correct analogical deduction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدُ: فَالمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِلِلِّسَانِ
الأنبابي
وَمِثَالُ البَحْثِ عَنِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصْدِيقِيِّ : البَحْتُ عَنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيِ القِيَاسِ كَقَوْلِنَا : العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرُ ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرِ حَادِثٌ وَهُمَا مَعْلُومَانِ تَصْدِيقِيَّانِ، بِأَنَّهُمَا إِذَا رُكْبًا عَلَى الوَجْهِ المَخْصُوصِ وَصَّلَ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصْدِيقِيٌّ كَقَوْلِنَا : العَالَمُ حَادِثُ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ تَوَقُفاً قَرِيباً لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ وَاسِطَةٍ: البَحْثُ عَنْ كُلِّ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيِ القِيَاسِ بِأَنَّهُ قَضِيَّةٌ أَوْ عَكْسُ قَضِيَّةٍ، أَوْ نَقِيضُ قَضِيَّةٍ ، أَوْ تَوَقُفاً بَعِيداً لِكَوْنِهِ بِوَاسِطَةِ تَوَقُفِهِ عَلَى القَضِيَّةِ: البَحْثُ عَمَّا ذُكِرَ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَوْضُوع وَالمَحْمُولِ.
And after: logic's relation to the mind is like grammar's relation to the tongue. Al-Anbabi: And an example of investigating the tasdiqiyyāt (assertoric statements) from the perspective that they lead to an unknown assertoric matter: the investigation of the two premises of the syllogism, like our saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated'—both are known assertorically—that when they are composed in the specific manner, their totality leads to an unknown assertoric matter, like our saying: 'The world is temporally originated.' And from the perspective of what that depends on, with a close dependency due to being without intermediary: the investigation of each of the two premises of the syllogism as being a proposition, or the converse of a proposition, or the contradictory of a proposition; or with a distant dependency due to being mediated by the dependency on the proposition: the investigation of what was mentioned in terms of subject and predicate.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and exemplary explanation of logic. It opens with the author's verse comparing logic to grammar: logic is to the inner faculty (jinan) what grammar is to the tongue (lisan). Just as grammar regulates speech externally, logic regulates thought internally. Then al-Anbabi provides a concrete example of investigating tasdiqiyyāt (assertoric statements) in logic. He explains that when we examine how known propositions lead to an unknown conclusion, we study the syllogism. His example: 'The world is changing' and 'Everything changing is temporally originated' are two known propositions. Composed correctly, they yield the unknown conclusion 'The world is temporally originated.' This illustrates the 'close dependency'—no intermediary between the premises and the conclusion. He then distinguishes between two types of investigation: (1) close dependency: examining the premises themselves as propositions, their converses, or their contradictories; (2) distant dependency: examining the subject and predicate of the proposition, since the syllogism depends on the proposition, which in turn depends on its subject and predicate. This breakdown shows the scope of logical inquiry into both immediate and mediate reasoning.

الجنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Logic is the grammar of the mind, ensuring correct internal reasoning.

Syllogistic Structure and Levels of Dependency
Illustration of syllogistic inquiry and its levels of dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between close and distant dependencies in syllogistic inquiry and apply this to the classical example of the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
Syllogistic inquiry splits into close dependency (premises as propositions) and distant dependency (subject and predicate).

After establishing logic's role, the author illustrates with a concrete syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated.' He then explains that the investigation of syllogisms involves two levels: a close dependency where we examine the premises as propositions, their converses or contradictories, and a distant dependency where we delve into the subject and predicate of the propositions themselves. This shows the depth of logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism example
What are the two premises and the conclusion? Premise 1: 'The world is changing.' Premise 2: 'Everything changing is temporally originated.' Conclusion: 'The world is temporally originated.'
2
Recognize the close dependency
Why is the dependency 'close'? (No intermediary between premises and conclusion). The investigation here looks at the premises as complete propositions, their converses, or contradictories.
3
Understand the distant dependency
What does investigating subject and predicate add? It goes deeper into the internal structure of the premise, examining the subject and predicate themselves.
4
Apply the breakdown
Given another syllogism (e.g., 'All intoxicants are forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden'), identify the two levels of dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدُ: فَالمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِلِلِّسَانِ
الأنبابي
وَمِثَالُ البَحْثِ عَنِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصْدِيقِيِّ : البَحْتُ عَنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيِ القِيَاسِ كَقَوْلِنَا : العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرُ ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرِ حَادِثٌ وَهُمَا مَعْلُومَانِ تَصْدِيقِيَّانِ، بِأَنَّهُمَا إِذَا رُكْبًا عَلَى الوَجْهِ المَخْصُوصِ وَصَّلَ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصْدِيقِيٌّ كَقَوْلِنَا : العَالَمُ حَادِثُ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ تَوَقُفاً قَرِيباً لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ وَاسِطَةٍ: البَحْثُ عَنْ كُلِّ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيِ القِيَاسِ بِأَنَّهُ قَضِيَّةٌ أَوْ عَكْسُ قَضِيَّةٍ، أَوْ نَقِيضُ قَضِيَّةٍ ، أَوْ تَوَقُفاً بَعِيداً لِكَوْنِهِ بِوَاسِطَةِ تَوَقُفِهِ عَلَى القَضِيَّةِ: البَحْثُ عَمَّا ذُكِرَ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَوْضُوع وَالمَحْمُولِ.
And after: logic's relation to the mind is like grammar's relation to the tongue. Al-Anbabi: And an example of investigating the tasdiqiyyāt (assertoric statements) from the perspective that they lead to an unknown assertoric matter: the investigation of the two premises of the syllogism, like our saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated'—both are known assertorically—that when they are composed in the specific manner, their totality leads to an unknown assertoric matter, like our saying: 'The world is temporally originated.' And from the perspective of what that depends on, with a close dependency due to being without intermediary: the investigation of each of the two premises of the syllogism as being a proposition, or the converse of a proposition, or the contradictory of a proposition; or with a distant dependency due to being mediated by the dependency on the proposition: the investigation of what was mentioned in terms of subject and predicate.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and exemplary explanation of logic. It opens with the author's verse comparing logic to grammar: logic is to the inner faculty (jinan) what grammar is to the tongue (lisan). Just as grammar regulates speech externally, logic regulates thought internally. Then al-Anbabi provides a concrete example of investigating tasdiqiyyāt (assertoric statements) in logic. He explains that when we examine how known propositions lead to an unknown conclusion, we study the syllogism. His example: 'The world is changing' and 'Everything changing is temporally originated' are two known propositions. Composed correctly, they yield the unknown conclusion 'The world is temporally originated.' This illustrates the 'close dependency'—no intermediary between the premises and the conclusion. He then distinguishes between two types of investigation: (1) close dependency: examining the premises themselves as propositions, their converses, or their contradictories; (2) distant dependency: examining the subject and predicate of the proposition, since the syllogism depends on the proposition, which in turn depends on its subject and predicate. This breakdown shows the scope of logical inquiry into both immediate and mediate reasoning.

Key Terms
القياس
التصديقية
قضية
عكس
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example syllogism, what does the author identify as the 'close dependency'?
A) The study of subject and predicate
B) The study of the premises as propositions
C) The study of the conclusion
D) The study of the world

Key Takeaway
Logical analysis of a syllogism extends from the premises to their internal components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Subject Matter of Logic: Close and Distant Dependencies
Author specifies subject matter and dependency classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between close and distant dependency in the context of logic and identify the subject matter of logic as conceptual and assertoric information under the aspect of conduction.

Core Idea
Logic studies information only as it conducts to the unknown, not as mental existents.

In this passage, the author clarifies what logic investigates and how its questions are structured. He distinguishes between rules that directly depend on propositions (close dependency) and those that depend on the parts of propositions (distant dependency). Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for grasping why logic is defined as a tool for acquiring knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of information
The author mentions 'conceptual information' and 'assertoric information'. Conceptual is about concepts (tasawwur), assertoric is about judgments (tasdiq). Note that both are studied in logic.
Conceptual information includes definitions and descriptions; assertoric includes propositions and syllogisms.
2
Understand close and distant dependency
Close dependency (tawaqquf qarib) refers to the direct reliance of the syllogism on its premises (propositions). Distant dependency (tawaqquf ba'id) refers to the reliance of propositions on their subject and predicate terms. Trace the chain: syllogism needs premises → premises need terms.
Close dependency concerns rules about propositions (e.g., conversion, contradiction). Distant dependency concerns rules about terms (e.g., genus, differentia).
3
Analyze the objection and response
An imagined objection says that logic has no predicate like 'conduction' to define it. The response: calling a conceptual known a 'definition' already implies conduction. This clarifies that logic's purpose is to lead from known to unknown.
This shows the circularity is only apparent; the definition is fulfilled by the very act of defining.
4
Identify the subject matter
The author states that the subject matter of logic is conceptual and assertoric information taken from the perspective of conduction. This excludes studying them as mental existences or as purely fictional (e.g., fangs of ghouls).
The logician's concern is limited to how information leads to new knowledge, not its ontological status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا؛ ثم ما سبق من كون الفصل أو الخاصة متوقفاً عليهما الموصل إذا لم يعرفا بكل منهما علی انفراده، فإن عرفا بكلِّ منهما على انفراده كانا من قبيل المعلومات التَّصوريَّة الموصلة بلا واسطة، والقواعد الباحثة عن المعلومات التصديقية المتوقف عليها الموصل للمجهول التصديقى توقُفاً قريباً القواعد المتعلّقة بالقضايا وأحكامها كالعكس، والتناقض، وكونها حمليَّاتٍ أو شرطيَّاتٍ. ووجه التَّوقف: أنَّ القياس الموصل للتصديق يتوقف على معرفة جزأيه، وهما: القضية الصُّغرى والكبرى، فلا بدَّ من معرفة القضيَّة بتعريفها وأحكامها والقواعد الباحثة عن المعلومات التصوريَّة، المتوقف عليها الموصل إلى التصديق توقُفاً بعيداً القواعد المتعلقة بالمحمول والموضوع، المذكورة في ضمن باب القضايا. ووجه التَّوقُف : أنَّ القياس الموصل متوقف على القضيَّة التي هي جزؤه، والقضيَّة متوقفة على جزئها الذي هو المحمول أو الموضوع. فإن قيل : ليس في المنطق مسألة محمولها الإيصال أو ما يتوقف عليه الإيصال حتّى يقال: «علم يُبحث فيه عن المعلومات التَّصوريَّة والتصديقية من حيث إيصالها ... إلخ». قيل : إنَّ الحكم على المعلوم التَّصوري بأنه حد أو رسم معناه : أنَّه موصل إلى المجهول التصوري بلا واسطة، وقس على هذا. ثمَّ إِنَّه قيَّد بقوله : (مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ ... إلخ) احترازاً عن البحث عنها، لا البحث عنها، لا من هذه الحيثيَّة ك كونها موجودة في الذهن، أو غير موجودة، وكونها ثابتة في نفس الأمر بقطع النظر عن اعتبار المعتبر. أو اعتباريَّةٌ محضة كـ : أنياب الأغوال، والقضايا الذهنية، فلا يبحث عنها المنطقي من هذه الجهات؛ إذ ليس غرضه متعلقاً بها . قوله : (وَمَوْضُوعُهُ : المَعْلُومَاتُ ... إلخ) أي : لأنَّ موضوع كلِّ علم ما يُبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذاتية؛ أي: المنسوبة إلى ذات المعروض نسبةً قويَّةً كـ المعلومات المذكورة مِنَ الحيثية المذكورة، والأعراض الذاتية كـ: الإيصال، وما يتوقف عليه الإيصال كـ الجنسيَّة والفصلية وكونها قضيَّةً أو عكس قضيَّةٍ، وحمليّة أو شرطيَّةً موجّهةً أو غير موجّهةٍ؛ إذ هي المبحوث عنها في المنطق . وإنَّما كان موضوع هذا الفنّ تلك المعلومات؛ لأنَّ المنطقي يبحث عن أحوالها التي هي الإيصال.
This; then what preceded regarding differentia or property being that on which the conduit depends when they are not known individually, but if they are known individually then they fall under conceptual information that conducts without intermediary. And the rules investigating the assertoric information on which the conduit to the unknown assertoric depends with close dependency are the rules related to propositions and their rulings, like conversion, contradiction, and whether they are categorical or conditional. The reason for the dependency: the syllogism leading to assenting depends on knowledge of its two parts, which are the minor and major premises; hence knowledge of the proposition—its definition and rulings—is necessary. And the rules investigating the conceptual information on which the conduit to assenting depends with distant dependency are the rules related to the predicate and subject, mentioned within the chapter on propositions. The reason for the dependency: the conducting syllogism depends on the proposition which is its part, and the proposition depends on its two parts, which are the predicate and subject. If it is said: there is no issue in logic whose predicate is 'conduction' or 'what depends on conduction' such that it is said: 'a science in which is investigated conceptual and assertoric information from the perspective of their conduction... etc.' It is said: the judgment on a conceptual known that it is a definition or description means that it conduces to the unknown conceptual without an intermediary, and analogously for other cases. Then he qualified with his statement: 'from the perspective that they conduct... etc.' as a precaution against investigating them not from this perspective, such as their being existent in the mind or non-existent, or their being fixed in themselves independent of a consideration, or purely mental constructs like 'the fangs of ghouls' and mental propositions; so the logician does not investigate them from these aspects since his purpose is not related to them. His statement: 'And its subject matter is conceptual and assertoric information... etc.' means: because the subject matter of every science is that in which it investigates its essential accidents, i.e., those strongly related to the essence of the substrate, like the mentioned information from the mentioned perspective; and the essential accidents are like conduction and what depends on conduction, such as being a genus or differentia, and being a proposition or the converse of a proposition, and being categorical or conditional, modal or non-modal; for these are what are investigated in logic. And the subject matter of this art is indeed that information because the logician investigates its states which are conduction.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological explanation of logic's subject matter and its division. The author first handles an objection regarding differentia and property: if they are known individually, they become conceptual information that conducts without intermediary (i.e., they are themselves the conduit). Then he distinguishes two sets of rules: (1) Rules investigating assertoric information with close dependency—these are rules about propositions, their conversion, contradiction, and types (categorical/conditional). The reason for the dependency is that the syllogism (which produces assent) depends on its two premises. (2) Rules investigating conceptual information with distant dependency—these are rules about the subject and predicate, discussed within the chapter on propositions. The reason for this distant dependency is that the syllogism depends on the proposition, and the proposition depends on its subject and predicate. He then addresses a potential objection: it might be argued that no logical problem has 'conduction' or 'what depends on conduction' as its predicate, so the definition of logic as being about information under the aspect of conduction is circular. The answer is that calling a conceptual known a 'definition' or 'description' implies it conducts to the unknown. Next, he explains the qualification 'from the perspective that they conduct'—this excludes investigations that are not from that perspective, such as whether the information exists in the mind, is mind-independent, or is purely mental (e.g., 'fangs of ghouls'). Finally, he defines the subject matter of logic as the two types of information (conceptual and assertoric) taken under the aspect of conduction. He justifies this by stating that every science's subject is that about which it investigates its essential accidents—here, conduction and its dependencies (like being a genus, differentia, proposition, etc.). Thus, the logician studies the states of these informations qua conducing to the unknown.

العوارض الذاتية
الإيصال
القريب والبعيد

What is Mantiq? The Grammar of the Mind
Author defines mantiq, its subject, fruit, and virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of mantiq as given in the text, explain its analogy to grammar, and describe its subject matter restricted to leading to unknowns under the condition of correctness.

Core Idea
Logic is to the mind as grammar is to the tongue.

The author opens this science by defining its object. He does not give a dry Greek definition but a living analogy: logic stands to the mind as grammar stands to speech. This lesson unpacks that analogy and the precise subject matter of logic as the art that leads from known information to unknown concepts or judgments, provided the path is correct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy used to define logic
Find the line that compares logic to grammar. What is the relationship between grammar and tongue? Then transfer that to logic and the mind.
The analogy is explicit: 'فَالْمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِ لِلَّسَانِ'.
2
Extract the subject matter of logic
The author says logic is about 'information from the aspect of the correctness of leading to an unknown matter'. Highlight the key words: 'صحة إيصالها' (correctness of leading). Why is 'correctness' specified?
The commentary explains this is to avoid the objection that the subject must be conceded.
3
Understand the two types of unknowns
The unknown can be conceptual (tasawwur) or assensual (tasdiq). Give a fiqh example: conceptual is understanding 'prayer', assensual is judging 'prayer is obligatory'.
This distinction is foundational for the whole science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(9) وَبَعْدُ: فَالمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِ لِلَّسَانِ. الباجوري (1) مِنْ حَيْثُ صِحَّةِ إِبْصَالِهَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٌّ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِي (١). (۳) - وَثَمَرَتُهُ : مَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْ : أَنَّهُ يَعْصِمُ الذَّهْنَ عَنِ الخَطَأ فِي الفِكْرِ. وَقِيلَ: مَعْرِفَةُ التَّألِيفَاتِ الصَّحِيحَةِ وَالفَاسِدَةِ. (٤) - وَفَضْلُهُ : فَوَقَانُهُ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ مِنَ العُلُومِ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنِهِ عَامَّ النَّفْعِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَبْحَثُ عَنْ كُلِّ عِلْمٍ تَصَوُّرِيٌّ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِيُّ (٢) ، وَهَذَا لَا يُنَافِي أَنَّ بَعْضَ بَاقِي العُلُومِ يَفُوقَهُ مِنْ حَيثَيَّةٍ أُخْرَى . الأنبابي (۲) . إلى المجهولات، وما يتوقَّفُ عليه هذا الإيصال، وهذه الأحوال هي العارضة للمعلومات التصورية والتصديقيَّة لذواتها، والفرق بين العوارض الذاتية وغيرها مذكور في القطب» و «حواشيه مع زياداتٍ أُخر متعلقة بالحد والموضوع المذكورين (۳). قوله : (مِنْ حَيْثُ صِحَّةِ إِبْصَالِهَا ... إلخ) أي : فموضوع المنطق مقيَّد بصحة الإيصال، لا بنفس الإيصال؛ حتّى يرد عليه : أنَّ قيد الموضوع من تتمته، لا يُبحث عنه في العِلم ؛ إذ لا بدَّ في كلِّ علم من كون موضوعه مسلّم الثبوت، والإيصال مبحوث عنه في هذا العلم، فيجب أن يكون حالاً عارضةً للموضوع، لا مسلَّم الثبوت . ومحصّل الجواب : أنَّ قيد الموضوع هو صحة الإيصال، لا الإيصال نفسه، وعلى هذا القياس نظائر هذا القيد في موضوعات العلوم. قوله : (وَقِيلَ : مَعْرِفَةُ التَّالِيفَاتِ... إلخ) في الحقيقة الثَّمرة الأولى متفرعة على هذه الثَّمرة . (۱) الشرشيمي: قوله : (مِنْ حَيْثُ صِحَّةِ إِبْصَالِهَا ... إلخ) أي : المبحوث عنها من حيث صحة الإيصال، لا من حيث ذاتها، وكذلك ومِن حيث ما يتوقف عليه التّوصُّل، أو المعلومات كما تقدَّم، ولعله تركه لعلمه ممَّا سبق، أو اقتصاراً على المقصود. (۲) الشرشيمي: قوله : ( يَبْحَثُ عَنْ كُلِّ عِلْمٍ تَصَوُّرِيٌّ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِي) إن قلت : أيُّ علم تصوري؛ لأنَّ جميع العلوم تصديقات؟ : كل علم فيه تصور وتصديق، فإنَّ علم التوحيد مثلاً إذا قيل فيه: «القُدْرَةُ وَاجِبَةٌ لِلهِ تَعَالَى، كان إدراك الطرفين تصوراً، وإدراك وقوع النسبة تصديقاً. قلت : ثمَّ ظهر أنَّ المراد بـ التَّصور» في العلوم: تصور تعاريف المفردات منها ؛ كـ تصور معنى الصَّلاة، والبيع، ... إلخ [1/ ١٤]، فإنَّ تعاريفها معلومات تصوريَّة توصل إلى أمر مجهول تصوري، وهو المعرّف، وأنَّ المراد بـ «التصديق» فيها : التصديق بالأقيسة الواقعة فيها ؛ كالقياس الحاصل في مقام الاستدلال على وجوب الصَّلاة بـ (أَقِيمُوا الصَّلوةَ ، فإنَّه يُقال : هذا أمر، وكلُّ أمرٍ للوجوب، وهكذا يقال في سائر الأدلة، وأما نفس الأحكام الفقهية كـ : كون فروض الوضوء ستَّة، أو كونه فرضاً، أو مندوباً ، فلا يُقال لها : معلومات تصوريَّة أو تصديقيَّة توصل ... إلخ» ؛ لأنَّها نفس المصدق بـ به، فهي نتيجة القياس والدليل، وإن كان إدراك مفرداتها تصوراً، وإدراك وقوع نسبتها تصديقاً؛ تأمل. (۳) انظر: مجموعة الشمسية) (١٥١/١) وما بعدها .
(9) After this: the relation of logic to the mind is like that of grammar to the tongue. Al-Bajuri: (1) From the aspect of the correctness of leading them to an unknown matter, conceptual or assensual. (3) And its fruit: what the author indicated: that it safeguards the mind from error in thinking. And it is said: knowledge of correct and corrupt compositions. (4) And its virtue: its superiority over other sciences by virtue of its general benefit; because it investigates every science, conceptual or assensual. And this does not contradict that some other sciences surpass it from another aspect. Al-Anbabi: (2) ... to the unknowns, and what this leading depends on, and these states are the accidents of conceptual and assensual information in themselves, and the difference between essential accidents and others is mentioned in 'al-Qutb' and its glosses with other additions related to the mentioned definition and subject. His words: 'From the aspect of the correctness of leading them ... etc.' meaning: the subject of logic is restricted to the correctness of leading, not the leading itself; such that it could be objected: the restriction of the subject is part of its completion, it is not investigated in the science; for in every science its subject must be conceded existence, and leading is investigated in this science, so it must be an accidental state of the subject, not conceded existence. The summary of the answer: the restriction of the subject is the correctness of leading, not the leading itself, and on this analogy are similar restrictions in the subjects of sciences. His words: 'And it is said: knowledge of compositions ... etc.' In reality the first fruit branches from this fruit. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His words: 'From the aspect of the correctness of leading them ... etc.' meaning: that which is investigated from the aspect of the correctness of leading, not from the aspect of its essence, and likewise from the aspect of what the attainment depends on, or the information as previously mentioned, and perhaps he left it due to knowledge from what preceded, or out of limitation to the intended. (2) Al-Shirshimi: His words: 'He investigates every science, conceptual or assensual.' If you say: What science is conceptual? For all sciences are assensuals? (Answer): Every science contains conception and assent, for the science of tawhid, for example, when it is said 'Power is obligatory for Allah the Exalted', the perception of the two terms is conception, and the perception of the occurrence of the relation is assent. I say: Then it became apparent that the intended meaning of 'conception' in the sciences is the conception of the definitions of the individual terms thereof; like the conception of the meaning of prayer, sale, etc., for their definitions are conceptual information leading to an unknown conceptual matter, which is the defined. And that the intended meaning of 'assent' therein is assent to the syllogisms occurring therein; like the syllogism obtained in the context of inference for the obligation of prayer by 'Establish prayer', for it is said: This is a command, and every command is for obligation, and similarly in other evidences. As for the actual juristic rulings, like the obligatory acts of ablution being six, or it being obligatory, or recommended, they are not called conceptual or assensual information leading ... etc., because they are the very things assented to, so they are the result of the syllogism and evidence, although perceiving their terms is conception and perceiving the occurrence of their relation is assent; consider. (3) See: Majmu'a al-Shamsiyya (1/151) and after.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the science of logic (al-mantiq) and explains its fruit (thamara) and virtue (fadl). The opening line draws an analogy: logic relates to the mind as grammar relates to the tongue. Just as grammar preserves the tongue from error in speech, logic preserves the mind from error in thinking (fikr). The author then clarifies that the subject matter of logic is 'information from the aspect of the correctness of leading to an unknown matter' — meaning logic studies the rules that ensure sound inference, whether the unknown is a concept (tasawwur) or a judgment (tasdiq). The fruit of logic is that it safeguards the mind from error in thought, or alternatively, it is the knowledge of correct and corrupt compositions (syllogisms). This is the immediate benefit. The virtue of logic is its generality: it benefits all sciences because it investigates the forms of reasoning used in every discipline. However, this does not mean logic is the noblest science in every respect; other sciences may surpass it in other aspects, such as subject matter or goal. The commentary by al-Anbabi elaborates on the definition: the subject of logic is the 'states' (ahwal) that are accidents of information, specifically essential accidents. He defends the qualification 'correctness of leading' — the subject is not the act of leading itself (which would require prior existence), but the correctness condition. Al-Shirshimi further clarifies that 'conception' in sciences refers to grasping definitions of terms (e.g., the definition of prayer or sale), while 'assent' refers to accepting syllogistic proofs (e.g., deducing obligation from a command). Actual juristic rulings, such as 'the obligatory acts of ablution are six', are not themselves informational premises but conclusions of reasoning. Thus, logic provides the framework for all sound inference, exemplified by fiqh reasoning.

Key Terms
المنطق
التصور
التصديق
صحة الإيصال

Key Takeaway
Logic is the guardian of correct reasoning, as grammar is the guardian of correct speech; its subject is the rules for sound inference to any unknown concept or judgment.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
4 min
The Fruit and Virtue of Logic
Author states fruit and virtue of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fruit (thamara) and virtue (fadl) of logic as presented in the text, and explain why logic's benefit is general across sciences.

Core Idea
Logic safeguards the mind and benefits all sciences.

Having defined logic, the author now states its purpose and its rank among sciences. The fruit of logic is twofold: it prevents error in thought, and it grants knowledge of correct and corrupt arguments. Its virtue lies in its universal applicability, as it examines the reasoning patterns used in every discipline, from fiqh to kalam. However, this generality does not mean logic is the noblest science in every respect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of the fruit
The text gives two statements: one from the author (safeguarding the mind) and one attributed to others (knowledge of correct and corrupt compositions). Note that the commentator says the first branches from the second.
These are complementary: the safeguard is achieved through knowledge of valid forms.
2
Understand the virtue statement
The virtue (fadl) is its superiority in generality: it benefits all sciences. Find the phrase 'عام النفع' and the reason: because it investigates every science conceptually and assensually.
Generality is a source of virtue, but not the only measure; other sciences may surpass in other aspects.
3
Reflect on the limitation
The text adds that logic's generality does not negate that some sciences are superior in other respects (e.g., higher subject matter). This prevents over-claiming.
This is a typical disclaimer in classifications of sciences.
