What Is Mantiq?
advanced
8 min
Defending the Metaphor: The Sky of Logic and Self-Reference
Bajuri explains the metaphor and refutes the self-reference objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the metaphorical expression 'sama' 'ilm al-mantiq' and evaluate Bajuri's refutation of the self-reference objection.

Core Idea
A book can be a ladder to its field if it consists of words, not the meanings of the entire field.

The matn says the book is a ladder to the sky of logic. But if the book is part of logic, doesn't that mean logic leads to itself? Bajuri's refutation teaches us a crucial distinction between words and meanings in logical definitions. This lesson unpacks the metaphor and the defense.

Guided Path
1
Parse the metaphor
Bajuri explains 'sama' 'ilm al-mantiq' as an idafat al-mushabbah bihi ila al-mushabbah: the sky (tool of comparison) is added to logic (the thing compared), meaning logic resembles the sky in loftiness.
This is a common rhetorical device in Arabic to express high status.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims tawsil al-shay' ila nafsihi: if the book is part of logic, and it is a ladder to logic, then logic leads to itself, which is absurd.
This is a logical fallacy: self-reference or circularity.
3
Examine Bajuri's first answer
Bajuri argues that the book consists of words (alfaz), not meanings (ma'ani). Since the science of logic is about meanings, the book is not essentially identical to logic, so no self-reference.
This distinction between lafz and ma'na is crucial in Islamic epistemology.
4
Examine Bajuri's second answer
Even if we consider the book as meanings, the intended meaning is that it leads to the other parts of logic, not to the entirety that includes itself. So the ladder leads to the rest, not the whole.
This is a subtle point: the book is a part, and it leads to the other parts, avoiding circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (يُرْقَى بِهِ ... إلخ) مُسْتَأنَفٌ اسْتِثْنَافاً بَيَانِيًّا، فَكَأَنَّ سَائِلاً قَالَ لَهُ: «مَا وَجْهُ تَسْمِيَتِهِ بِالسُّلَّم؟» فَقَالَ: «يُرْقَى بِهِ. إلخ . وَالصَّمِيرُ يَرْجِعُ لِلمُؤَلِّفِ الَّذِي رَجَعَ إِلَيْهِ الضَّمِيرُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : سَمَّيْتُهُ ، وَكَذَلِكَ الضَّمَائِرُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَأَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصاً ... إلخ»، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنَ الشَّرْحِ الصَّغِيرِ» لِلشَّيْخِ المَلْوِيِّ . وَيَصِحُ رُجُوعُ ذَلِكَ لِـ «السُّلَّمِ المُتَقَدِّمِ كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ فِي الشَّرْحِ الكَبِيرِ) ، لَكِنْ يَتَعَيَّنُ أَنْ يُرَادَ بِهِ المُسَمَّى لَا الاِسْمُ ، كَمَا هُوَ المُرَادُ بِهِ فِيمَا سَبَقَ ، فَيَكُونُ فِيهِ اسْتِخْدَامُ، لَكِنَّ الأَوَّلَ أَوْلَى كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى . وَقَوْلُهُ : (سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ) أَيْ : عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ الشَّبِيهِ بِالسَّمَاءِ فِي العُلُوِّ ، فَإِضَافَةُ «سَمَاءِ» لِمَا بَعْدَهُ مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّه . لا يُقَالُ : يَلْزَمُ عَلَى كَلَام المُصَنِّفِ تَوْصِيلُ الشَّيْءِ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ هَذَا المُؤَلَّفَ بَعْضُ المَنْطِقِ، وَقَدْ جَعَلَهُ مُوصلاً لِعِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ، المُشْتَمِل عَلَى ذَلِكَ البَعْضِ . لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ هَذَا المُؤَلَّفَ أَلْفَاظٌ لَا مَعَانٍ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ مَا ذُكِرَ، وَعَلَى تَسْلِيم أَنَّهُ مَعَانٍ فَالمُرَادُ : أَنَّهُ يُرْقَى بِهِ لِمَا عَدَاهُ مِنْ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ، لَا لِجَمِيعِهِ الشَّامِلِ لَهُ.
His statement: (by which it is ascended ... etc.) is a resumed explanatory resumption, as if a questioner asked him: 'What is the reason for naming it al-Sullam?' So he said: 'By which it is ascended ... etc.' The pronoun refers to the author to whom the pronoun in his statement 'I named it' referred, and likewise the pronouns in his statement 'and that it be pure ... etc.', as taken from al-Sharh al-Saghir by al-Malwi. And it is valid for that to refer to the 'al-Sullam' mentioned earlier, as mentioned in al-Sharh al-Kabir, but it must be intended as the named thing, not the name, as was intended previously, thus there is a use [of the word for its meaning], but the first is better, as is not hidden. And his statement: (the sky of the science of logic) means the science of logic which resembles the sky in loftiness, so the addition of 'sky' to what follows is an addition of the analogized-to to the analogized. It cannot be said: There follows from the author's words that a thing leads to itself, because this authored work is a part of logic, and he made it a means of ascending to the science of logic which includes that part. For we say: It is not hidden that this authored work consists of words, not meanings, so what was mentioned does not follow. And on the assumption that it is meanings, the intended meaning is that it is ascended by it to the rest of the science of logic, not to its entirety that includes it.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's line 'yurqa bihi sama' 'ilm al-mantiq' meaning 'by which the sky of the science of logic is ascended.' The commentator first explains that the phrase is an isti'naf bayani, a clarification resumption answering a hypothetical question why the book is named 'ladder'. Then he discusses pronoun reference: the pronouns in the matn refer to the author (the matn writer) or to the book itself. He notes that it is possible to refer to the book as the named thing, not the name, but the first interpretation (referring to the author) is preferable. Then he explains the metaphor: 'sama' 'ilm al-mantiq' means logic is like the sky in loftiness; the construct is 'idafat al-mushabbah bihi ila al-mushabbah' (the analogized-to is added to the analogized). Then an objection is raised: If the book is a ladder to logic, but the book itself is part of logic, then it would be like leading the thing to itself. The answer is twofold: First, the book consists of words, not meanings, so it is not essentially identical to logic (which is a science of meanings). Second, even if it is considered meanings, the intended meaning is that it leads to the other parts of logic, not to the entire thing that includes it. This careful distinction preserves the metaphor without logical fallacy.

Key Terms
إِضَافَة المُشَبَّه بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّه
تَوْصِيل الشَّيْء إِلَى نَفْسِهِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first argument Bajuri gives to refute the self-reference objection?
A) The book is a ladder only to the sky, not to logic.
B) The book consists of words, not meanings.
C) The book is the entire science of logic.
D) The metaphor should be taken literally.

Key Takeaway
Bajuri's defense of the metaphor shows how distinguishing between words and meanings, as well as between part and whole, can resolve apparent self-reference in definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'al-fiqh huwa al-'ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyya al-'amaliyya'. Is this definition self-referential? Apply Bajuri's method: determine if the definiens (al-'ilm) is essentially the same as the definiendum (al-fiqh) or if it leads to a part. Explain your reasoning.
?
Find another instance in a classical text where a metaphorical expression is criticized for self-reference. Use Bajuri's two answers to defend it and write a short refutation.

Understanding 'Fasl': Linguistic vs. Terminological Meaning
Author defines 'fasl' linguistically and terminologically as a chapter heading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic and terminological meanings of 'fasl' in Islamic logical texts and identify its role as a chapter heading.

Core Idea
'Fasl' in logic is not the differentia; it is a section marker.

This passage provides a dual definition of the term 'fasl' as it appears in the text. The author first gives its linguistic meaning as a barrier, then its technical meaning as a specific chapter heading. The commentator expands on how the term relates to the author's words, clarifying the scope of reference. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting later discussions that use 'fasl' as a structural element.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find where the passage gives the linguistic definition (الحاجز بين الشيئين) and the terminological definition (الألفاظ المخصوصة الدالة على المعاني المخصوصة).
The linguistic meaning is broader; the technical meaning is specific to section headings.
2
Compare the meanings
Note that the linguistic meaning is a physical barrier, while the technical meaning is a verbal marker that introduces a specific topic.
3
Analyze the commentator's expansion
The commentator interprets 'fi jawaz al-ishtighal bihi' as 'fi dal dhalika' and explains that the relation between 'fasl' and 'dal' is one of specific within general.
This shows how the commentator clarifies the reference of the author's phrase.
4
Recognize the usage
Understand that 'fasl' here is a section heading, not the logical differentia (al-fasl al-muqawwim) used in definitions.
Confusing these two leads to misinterpretation of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْلٌ) هُوَ فِي اللُّغَةِ : «الحَاجِزُ بَيْنَ الشَّيْتَيْنِ . وَفِي الاِصْطِلَاحِ : الأَلْفَاظُ المَخْصُوصَةُ الدَّالَّة عَلَى المَعَانِي المَخْصُوصَةِ كَسَائِرِ أَسْمَاءِ التَّرَاجِمِ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (فِي جَوازِ الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ أَيْ: فِي دَالٌ ذَلِكَ، وَالظَّرْفِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ مِنْ ظَرْفِيَّةِ الخَاصٌ فِي العَامّ؛ لِأَنَّ الفَصْلَ خَاصٌ بِالأَلْفَاظِ الَّتِي ذَكَرَهَا المُصَنِّفُ، وَالدَّالُ عَامٌ لَهَا وَلِلأَلْفَاظِ الَّتِي ذَكَرَهَا غَيْرُهُ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ غَيْرُ ذَلِكَ .
His statement: (A chapter) is in language: 'the barrier between two things'. And in terminology: the specific words that indicate specific meanings like other names of sections, as known from what has passed. And his statement: (on the permissibility of engaging in it) means: on that which indicates that, and the circumstantial relation then is of the specific within the general; because the chapter is specific to the words that the author mentioned, and the indication is general for them and for the words that others mentioned. And something other than that is possible.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'fasl' as used in the text. It first gives the linguistic meaning: 'al-hajiz bayn al-shay'ayn' (the barrier between two things). The commentator notes that this is the nominal sense (the barrier) as opposed to the verbal sense (the act of separating). Then it gives the terminological meaning: 'the specific words that indicate specific meanings'—i.e., 'fasl' in this context is a chapter heading that introduces a particular topic. The commentator then explains the phrase 'fi jawaz al-ishtighal bihi' (on the permissibility of engaging in it) by interpreting it as 'fi dal dhalika' (on that which indicates that), where 'dhalika' refers to the permissibility. The circumstantial relation (zarfiyya) is of the specific within the general: the chapter heading is specific to the author's words, while 'indication' (dal) is general for both the author's and others' words. The commentator acknowledges that other interpretations are possible.

Key Terms
فصل (fasl): Linguistically: barrier between two things; terminologically: a section heading that indicates a specific topic. [Note: No significant shift—this term is stable across the tradition.]
لغة (lugha): Language; the common linguistic usage as opposed to technical terminology.
اصطلاح (istilah): Technical terminology; the specific meaning assigned by scholars in a given discipline.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the terminological meaning of 'fasl'?
A) The barrier between two things
B) The specific words that indicate specific meanings
C) The act of separating
D) A type of logical definition

Key Takeaway
Knowing whether a term is used in its linguistic or technical sense is essential for accurate interpretation of scholarly texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find the term 'fasl' in the beginning of a matn from another Islamic science (e.g., a fiqh text) and determine whether it is used in its linguistic sense or as a chapter heading.
?
Draw a diagram showing the relationship between 'fasl' (specific) and 'dal' (general) as explained by the commentator.

Classification of Logic and the Ruling on the First Type
Bajuri divides mantiq into two divisions and rules on the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two divisions of mantiq and explain the ruling of farḍ kifāya for the sound type, including its condition and rationale.

Core Idea
Sound logic is a communal obligation because defending kalam depends on it.

Mantiq (logic) is not a monolithic discipline. Some forms are contaminated with philosophical errors leading to disbelief, while others remain sound and necessary. In this passage, Bajuri divides mantiq into two parts and clarifies the ruling on the first: it is permissible and even obligatory—but only under certain conditions. Let's understand this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Find the two divisions
Locate where Bajuri says 'علم المنطق قسمان'. Identify what distinguishes the first division from the second.
The second division is implied but not described in this passage.
2
Characterize the first division
List the adjectives used: 'خالٍ عن ضلالات الفلاسفة المكفرة' and note the examples given (texts like al-Sanusi's Mukhtasar, Isaghuji, etc.).
The key is that it is free from misguidance that leads to disbelief.
3
Extract the ruling
The text says 'لا خلاف في جواز الاشتغال به، بل هو فرض كفاية'. Understand the two levels: permissibility and obligation.
Farḍ kifāya means if enough people study it, the rest are absolved.
4
Follow the reasoning
The author gives a proof: refuting doubts in kalam is farḍ kifāya, and whatever that depends on is also farḍ kifāya. Write down the logical chain.
Notice the conditional: this applies only when one is not independent of it due to natural intelligence.
5
Note the historical exception
The text states the Companions and early imams did not need it. Why? Because of their strong intellects and sound dispositions.
This shows that the obligation is context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ عِلْمَ المَنْطِقِ قِسْمَانِ: (١) - أَحَدُهُمَا: مَا هُوَ خَالٍ عَنْ ضَلَالَاتِ الفَلَاسِفَةِ المُكَفِّرَةِ وَغَيْرِهَا كَ: المَذْكُورِ فِي هَذَا المَتْنِ، وَمُخْتَصَرِ السَّنُوسِيِّ، وَإِيْسَاغُوجِي، وَمُخْتَصَرِ ابْنِ عَرَفَة وَتَألِيفِ الكَاتِبِيِّ وَالخُوْنَجِيِّ وَالسَّعْدِ وَغَيْرِهِمْ مِنَ المُتَأَخْرِينَ. وَهَذَا القِسْمُ لَا خِلَافَ فِي جَوَازِ الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ، بَلْ هُوَ فَرْضُ كِفَايَةٍ عَلَى أَهْلِ كُلِّ إِقْلِيمٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ رَدُّ الشُّكُوكِ فِي عِلْمِ الكَلامِ، وَهُوَ فَرْضُ كِفَايَةٍ، وَمَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، وَمَحَلُّ ذَلِكَ إِذَا لَمْ يَسْتَغْنِ عَنْهُ بِجُودَةِ الذَّهْنِ وَصِحَّةِ الطَّبْعِ كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّنُوسِيُّ فِي شَرْحِ مُخْتَصَرِهِ وَابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ وَغَيْرُهُمَا ، وَلِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَحْتَجْ إِلَيْهِ الصَّحَابَةُ وَالتَّابِعُونَ وَالأَئِمَّةُ المُجْتَهِدُونَ وَأَصْحَابُهُمْ.
And know that the science of logic is two divisions: One of them: what is free from the misguidance of the philosophers that lead to disbelief and others, like what is mentioned in this matn, and the Mukhtasar of al-Sanusi, and Isaghuji, and the Mukhtasar of Ibn 'Arafa, and the writings of al-Katibi, al-Khunaji, al-Sa'd, and others from the later scholars. This division there is no dispute regarding the permissibility of occupying oneself with it, rather it is a communal obligation upon the people of every region; because the refutation of doubts in the science of kalam depends on it, and that is a communal obligation, and whatever a communal obligation depends on is a communal obligation. And that applies when one is not independent of it due to sharpness of mind and soundness of natural disposition, as al-Sanusi stated in his commentary on his Mukhtasar, and Ibn Ya'qub and others. And for that reason, the Companions, the Followers, and the mujtahid imams and their companions did not need it.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and ruling passage where the commentator (Bajuri) clarifies the two divisions of the science of logic. He first defines the first division as that which is free from the misguidance of the philosophers (especially those that lead to disbelief), citing specific works like the matn (al-Sullam al-murunq), the Mukhtasar of al-Sanusi, Isaghuji, etc. He then declares that there is no dispute about the permissibility of studying this type; rather, it is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya) on the people of every region. The proof is that it is needed for refuting doubts in kalam, which itself is a communal obligation, and whatever a communal obligation depends on also becomes a communal obligation. However, he qualifies this by noting that it applies only when one cannot rely on natural intelligence and sound disposition, as stated by al-Sanusi and Ibn Ya'qub. He points out that the Companions and early imams did not need it due to their strong natural intellects.

Key Terms
قسمان
خالٍ عن ضلالات الفلاسفة
فرض كفاية
رد الشكوك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on studying the first type of mantiq (free from philosophical misguidance)?
A) It is forbidden (haram).
B) It is permissible (mubah) but not recommended.
C) It is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya).
D) It is a personal obligation (farḍ 'ayn).

Key Takeaway
The sound type of mantiq, free from philosophical misguidance, is a communal obligation for the people of each region because it is needed to defend the science of kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why Restrict 'al-Ḥādith' in the Division of Knowledge?
Author defends the addition of 'al-hadith' to exclude God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author added the qualifier 'al-ḥādith' and evaluate the objections against it using the rules of definition.

Core Idea
Definitions must exclude impossible attributes of God even if a minority opinion might include them.

In logic, definitions must be precise and exclusive. The author of the matn divided knowledge into necessary and acquired, but added a qualifier 'contingent' to avoid implying compulsion in God's knowledge. In this commentary, al-Bajuri defends that qualifier against an objection, showing the importance of anticipating possible misinterpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and qualifier
Find the term being defined and the added restriction 'al-ḥādith' in the passage. The definiendum is knowledge (al-ʿilm) divided into necessary and acquired, but the matn adds 'al-ḥādith' to necessary knowledge.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that the word 'anwāʿ' (types) already excludes God's knowledge because God has no types. Why then add 'al-ḥādith'?
3
Reconstruct the author's rebuttal
The author responds that some Sunni theologians hold that God's knowledge is multiple by the multiplicity of the known. If that multiplicity were by species, then 'anwāʿ' might not exclude it. However, the correct view is that it is by individuals, not species. Thus 'anwāʿ' alone could be ambiguous, so 'al-ḥādith' is needed.
4
Note the better answer
The author gives a clearer justification: the qualifier is added for clarification, especially for those unaware that God's knowledge is not diverse in species. This ensures the definition is understood correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الضَّرُورِيُّ فَهُوَ وَإِنْ كَانَ مَعْنَاهُ - أَعْنِي : مَا لَمْ يَحْصُلْ عَنْ نَظَرٍ وَاسْتِدْلَالٍ» - صَحِيحاً فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى، لَكِنَّ إِطْلَاقَهُ عَلَى عِلْمِهِ تَعَالَى فِيهِ إِيهَامُ مُقَارَنَتِهِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ؛ لِإِطْلَاقِ الضَّرُورِيِّ عَلَى مَا اقْتَضَتْهُ الضَّرُورَةُ، وَذَلِكَ مُسْتَحِيلٌ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى . فَإِنْ قِيلَ: لَا حَاجَةَ لِزِيَادَةِ المُصَنِّفِ لِهَذَا القَيْدِ؛ لِخُرُوجِ عِلْمِهِ تَعَالَى بِلَفْظِ الْأَنْوَاعِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا أَنْوَاعَ لَهُ. أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ قَيَّدَ بِذَلِكَ ، لِيُخْرِجَ عِلْمَهُ تَعَالَى حَتَّى عَلَى قَوْلِ بَعْضِ أَكَابِرِ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ : أَنَّ عِلْمَهُ تَعَالَى يَتَعَدَّدُ بِتَعَدُّدِ المَعْلُومٍ. وَفِيهِ : أَنَّ هَذَا يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ القَائِلَ بِذَلِكَ يَقُولُ : بِأَنَّ تَعَدُّدَ العِلْمِ بِتَعَدُّدِ المَعْلُومِ تَعَدُّدٌ بِالنَّوْعِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، بَلْ يَقُولُ : بِأَنَّ تَعَدُّدَهُ بِذَلِكَ تَعَدُّدُ بِالشَّخْصِ فِي مَا يَظْهَرُ . فَالأَوْلَى الجَوَابُ : بِأَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ أَرَادَ الإِيضَاحَ، فَزَادَ القَيْدَ المَذْكُورَ تَصْرِيحاً بِالمَقْصُودِ لَا سِيَّمَا بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِمَنْ لَمْ يَعْلَمُ عَدَمَ تَنَوُّع عِلْمِهِ تَعَالَى، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ .
As for the necessary [knowledge], its meaning – i.e., that which is not obtained through investigation and inference – is correct when applied to Him, Exalted. However, applying it to His knowledge implies its association with necessity, because 'necessary' is applied to what is compelled by necessity, and that is impossible with respect to Him, Exalted. If it is said: There is no need for the author to add this restriction, because His knowledge is excluded by the word 'types', since He has no types. The answer: The author restricted it in order to exclude His knowledge even according to the statement of some leading Sunnis that His knowledge is multiplied by the multiplicity of the known. But this implies that the proponent of that view holds that the multiplicity of knowledge by the multiplicity of the known is a multiplicity by species, which is not the case. Rather, he holds that its multiplicity thereby is a multiplicity by individual, as appears. The better answer is that the author intended clarification, so he added the aforementioned restriction as an explicit statement of the intent, especially for someone who does not know the non-diversification of His knowledge. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refinement of a definition. The author (Bajuri) is commenting on a matn that divides knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (naẓarī). He argues that applying the term 'necessary' to God's knowledge is problematic because it suggests compulsion, which is impossible for God. He then addresses an objection: why did the author of the matn add the restriction 'al-ḥādith' (contingent)? The objector says it is unnecessary because God's knowledge has no types, so the word 'anwā'' already excludes it. The rebuttal is that some Sunni scholars hold that God's knowledge is multiple according to the multiplicity of known things, so the word 'anwā'' might not be sufficient if they mean species-multiplicity. However, the correct view is that they mean individual-multiplicity, not species. Therefore, the better justification is that the author added the restriction for clarity, especially for those unaware that God's knowledge is not diverse in species. The passage thus defends the precision of the matn's wording.

Key Terms
الضَّرُورِي
الأَنْوَاع
تَعَدُّدُ بِالشَّخْصِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author add the restriction 'al-ḥādith' to the division of knowledge?
A) To exclude necessary knowledge.
B) To exclude God's knowledge even according to a minority view that His knowledge is multiple by individuals.
C) To include acquired knowledge.
D) To avoid the implication of compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The addition of 'al-ḥādith' in the division of knowledge ensures that God's knowledge is excluded, even against a minority view that His knowledge is multiple, by clarifying that the multiplicity is by individuals, not species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the definition of 'knowledge' in a kalam text, identify a qualifier that excludes divine attributes and explain why it is necessary.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
Definition of Tasawwur: Perception Without External Assent
Al-Bajuri defines tasawwur and distinguishes its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given perception is tasawwur or tasdiq according to al-Bajuri's criteria, and distinguish between the specific and general meanings of tasawwur.

Core Idea
Tasawwur includes not only simple concepts but also relational perceptions without assent.

In this passage, al-Bajuri expands on the definition of tasawwur (conception) from the matn. He explains that tasawwur is not merely the perception of a single term; it encompasses several types of perception that do not involve assent to an external relation. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing tasawwur from tasdiq (assent), which is the other fundamental division of knowledge. Let's break down his analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition from the matn
The matn states: 'Perception of a singular is called tasawwur; perception of a relation is called tasdiq.' Note this initial pairing as the starting point.
The matn is the base verse al-Bajuri is commenting on.
2
Understand the expansion of 'perception of a singular'
Al-Bajuri explains that 'perception of the singular' means perception not connected to an external relation with assent. This includes three broad cases: (1) no relation at all, (2) non-external relation, (3) external relation without assent.
This expansion is key: tasawwur is broader than just concepts.
3
Recognize the types of non-external relations
Non-external relations are three: linguistic (affirmation/negation in a proposition), additive (e.g., sonship in 'Zaid ibn Amr'), and descriptive (e.g., 'rational animal'). All these are considered tasawwur.
These relations are internal to language or mental association, not judgments about reality.
4
Distinguish the two usages of tasawwur
Tasawwur has a specific meaning (as defined here, a division of knowledge opposed to tasdiq) and a general meaning (synonymous with knowledge itself, including both tasawwur and tasdiq).
Be careful: context determines which meaning is intended.
5
Test your understanding with examples
Given a statement like 'Zaid is standing', determine if each part is tasawwur or tasdiq. The concept 'Zaid' alone is tasawwur; the relation 'Zaid is standing' with assent is tasdiq; without assent it is tasawwur.
Practice applying the criteria to concrete cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِدْرَاكُ مُفْرَدٍ: «تَصَوُّراً» عُلِمَ وَدَرْكُ نِسْبَةٍ بِـ «تَصْدِيقِ وُسِمْ
قَوْلُهُ : (إِدْرَاكُ مُفْرَدٍ ... إلخ) المُرَادُ بِإِدْرَاكِ المُفْرَدِ : الإِدْرَاكُ الَّذِي لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقْ بِالنِّسْبَةِ الخَارِجِيَّةِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الإِذْعَانِ :
- بِأَنْ لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقْ بِنِسْبَةٍ أَصْلاً ، وَذَلِكَ : إِدْرَاكُ المَوْضُوعِ وَحْدَهُ ، وَإِدْرَاكُ المَحْمُولِ وَحْدَهُ، وَإِدْرَاكُهُمَا دُونَ النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا
أَوْ تَعَلَّقَ بِنِسْبَةٍ غَيْرِ خَارِجِيَّةٍ ، وَذَلِكَ : إِدْرَاكُ النِّسْبَةِ الكَلَامِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ ثُبُوتُ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الإِثْبَاتِ فِي القَضِيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ، وَعَلَى وَجْهِ النَّفْيِ فِي القَضِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَتْ إِنْشَائِيَّةً أَوْ خَبَرِيَّةً، وَإِدْرَاكُ النِّسْبَةِ الإِضَافِيَّةِ كَالنِّسْبَةِ فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدُ بْنُ عَمْرٍو وَهِيَ بُنُوَّةُ زَيْدٍ لِعَمْرٍو، وَإِدْرَاكُ النِّسْبَةِ التَّفْسِيرِيَّةِ كَالنِّسْبَةِ فِي قَوْلِكَ : حَيَوَانٌ نَاطِقٌ وَهِيَ كَوْنُ الثَّانِي صِفَةً لِلأَوَّلِ .
أَوْ تَعَلَّقَ بِنِسْبَةٍ خَارِجِيَّةٍ لَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الإِذْعَانِ؛ بِأَنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَابِلاً وَمُسَلِّماً لَهَا عَلَى مَا يَأْتِي فِي تَفْسِيرِ الإِذْعَانِ. فَكُلُّ مَا ذُكِرَ دَاخِلٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ التَّصَوُّرِ ، وَقَدْ أَبْلَغَ بَعْضُهُمْ صُوَرَهُ إِلَى خَمْسٍ وَعِشْرِينَ صُورَةً، فَلْتُرَاجَع .
قَوْلُهُ : (تَصَوُّراً) عُلِمَ أَيْ : عُلِمَ بِالتَّصَوُّرِ ؛ بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ سُمِّيَ بِهِ ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ التَّصَوُّرَ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى قِسْمٌ مِنَ العِلْمِ، وَأَمَّا بِمَعْنَى : حُصُولِ صُورَةِ الشَّيْءِ فِي النَّفْسِ ، فَهُوَ مُرَادِفٌ لِلْعِلْمِ الشَّامِلِ لِلتَّصَوُّرِ بِذَلِكَ المَعْنَى وَلِلتَّصْدِيقِ ؛ فَتَحَصَّلَ أَنَّ التَّصَوُّرَ لَهُ اسْتِعْمَالَانِ - كَمَا قَالَهُ فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ (۳) ـ:
- أَحَدُهُمَا : اسْتِعْمَالُهُ فِي المَعْنَى الأَخَصِّ ، وَهُوَ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ .
- وَالثَّانِي : اسْتِعْمَالُهُ فِي المَعْنَى الأَعَمِّ، وَهُوَ مَا تَقَدَّمَ، فَلَا تَغْفَلْ.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَدَرْكُ نِسْبَةٍ ... إلخ) الدَّرْكُ : اسْمُ مَصْدَرٍ بِمَعْنَى الإِدْرَاكِ . وَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ مِمَّا مَرَّ : أَنَّ النِّسْبَةَ الكَلَامِيَّةَ هِيَ : «ثُبُوتُ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الإِثْبَاتِ أَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ النَّفْيِ» ، بِخِلَافِ النِّسْبَةِ الخَارِجِيَّةِ ، فَإِنَّهَا : «وُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ الثُّبُوتِ أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوعِهِ» وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ : أَنَّ إِدْرَاكَ النِّسْبَةِ الكَلَامِيَّةِ يُسَمَّى : تَصَوُّراً.
Perception of a singular: it is called 'tasawwur'; and perception of a relation is named 'tasdiq'.
His statement: ('Perception of a singular... etc.) What is meant by 'perception of the singular' is: the perception that is not connected to an external relation in the mode of assent, either by not being connected to any relation at all, and that is: perception of the subject alone, perception of the predicate alone, and perception of both without the relation between them; or it is connected to a non-external relation, and that is: perception of the linguistic relation which is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in the mode of affirmation in the affirmative proposition, and in the mode of negation in the negative proposition, whether it is performative or informative; and perception of the additive relation like the relation in your saying: Zaid, son of Amr, which is Zaid's sonship to Amr; and perception of the descriptive relation like the relation in your saying: 'rational animal', which is the second being a description of the first.
Or it is connected to an external relation not in the mode of assent, i.e., not being accepting and submitting to it, as will come in the explanation of assent. So everything that has been mentioned is included in the definition of tasawwur. Some people have extended its forms to twenty-five forms, so let it be consulted.
His statement: ('Tasawwur' as known) i.e., it is known by 'tasawwur', meaning it is named by it. It is not hidden that tasawwur in this sense is a division of knowledge. As for its meaning: 'the attainment of the form of a thing in the mind', it is synonymous with knowledge which includes tasawwur in that sense and tasdiq. Thus it follows that tasawwur has two usages, as stated in Sharh al-Shamsiyya: one is its usage in the specific sense, which the author mentioned; the second is its usage in the general sense, which preceded. So do not be heedless.
His statement: ('And perception of a relation... etc.) 'al-dark' is a verbal noun meaning perception. You have already known from what has passed that the linguistic relation is: 'the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in the mode of affirmation or in the mode of negation', unlike the external relation, for it is: 'the occurrence of that affirmation or the non-occurrence of it.' And it has preceded that perception of the linguistic relation is called 'tasawwur'.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion. It begins by quoting the matn verse: 'Perception of a singular is called tasawwur; perception of a relation is called tasdiq.' The commentary then explains what 'perception of a singular' means. It is not simply perception of one thing; rather, it is perception that does not involve an external relation with assent (idh'an). This excludes perception of an external relation when one assents to it (which is tasdiq). The author then divides the perception that qualifies as tasawwur into two broad categories: (1) perception that is not connected to any relation at all—this includes perceiving the subject alone, the predicate alone, or both without the relation between them; (2) perception that is connected to a non-external relation—this includes three sub-types: the linguistic relation (the relation of affirmation or negation in a proposition, whether performative or informative), the additive relation (like the relation of sonship in 'Zaid ibn Amr'), and the descriptive relation (like the relation of description in 'rational animal'). Then a third category is added: perception that is connected to an external relation but not in the mode of assent (i.e., the person does not accept or submit to it). All these types are included under tasawwur. The commentator notes that some scholars have enumerated 25 forms of tasawwur (al-Sabban is mentioned in the margin). After this, the passage clarifies the word 'tasawwur' in the matn. It explains that in this context, tasawwur is the specific sense (one of the two divisions of knowledge), as opposed to the general sense which is synonymous with knowledge itself (including both tasawwur and tasdiq). This dual usage is common in the tradition and must be kept in mind. Finally, it clarifies that 'dark' means idrak (perception), and distinguishes between the linguistic relation (which is the relation of predicate to subject in speech) and the external relation (the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation in reality). Perception of the linguistic relation is still called tasawwur, not tasdiq. The examples used are from Arabic grammar (Zaid ibn Amr) and logic (rational animal).

Key Terms
التصوّر
التصديق
الإدراك
النسبة الخارجية
النسبة الكلامية
الإذعان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri's commentary, which of the following is NOT considered a tasawwur?
A) Perceiving the subject 'Zaid' alone
B) Perceiving the predicate 'standing' alone
C) Perceiving the relation in the statement 'Zaid is standing' with full assent
D) Perceiving the relation in the statement 'Zaid ibn Amr'

Key Takeaway
Tasawwur is perception without assent to an external relation, and it has two meanings: a specific division of knowledge and a general synonym for knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining al-Nazari and al-Daruri
Author defines nazari and daruri, then enumerates three types of necessary propositions: awwaliyat, hadsiyat, tajribiyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-nazari and al-daruri and explain why induction and analogy are included under al-nazari.

Core Idea
Theoretical knowledge includes more than syllogisms — induction and analogy are also tools of discovery.

In Islamic logic, knowledge is divided into the theoretical (nazari) and the necessary (daruri). This lesson clarifies the scope of theoretical knowledge and shows why the commentator broadens it beyond formal syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of nazari
Find where the commentator defines nazari as 'that which requires reflection (ta'amul)'. Note that it includes definition, syllogism, induction, and analogy.
2
Notice the broadening of nazari
The commentator warns against restricting nazari to technical reasoning (al-nazar al-istilahi). He explicitly includes induction and analogy.
3
Understand daruri as the opposite
Daruri is defined as 'that which does not require reflection'. The commentator clarifies that 'aks' here is linguistic opposition, not technical conversion.
4
Check what daruri encompasses
The commentator mentions that daruri includes awwaliyat, hadsiyat, and tajribiyat, which are explained in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالنَّظَرِيُّ : مَا احْتَاجَ لِلتَّأَمُّلِ وَعَكْسُهُ هُوَ الضَّرُورِيُّ الجَلِي. أَيْ: بِأَنْ يُرِيدُوا بِهِ مَا يُوصِلُ إِلَى المَجْهُولِ مِنْ تَعْرِيفٍ أَوْ قِيَاسٍ أَوِ اسْتِقْرَاءِ أَوْ تَمْثِيلٍ، لَا مَا يَخُصُّ التَّعْرِيفَ وَالقِيَاسَ، كَمَا قَدْ يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنَ التَّعْبِيرِ بِـ «النَّظَرِيِّ»، فَإِنَّ المُتَبَادَرَ أَنَّهُ مَنْسُوبٌ لِلنَّظَرِ الإصطلاحِي فَقَط، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، بَلْ هُوَ مَنْسُوبٌ لِلنَّظَرِ بِمَعْنَى يَعُمُّ الاِصْطِلَاحِيَّ وَمَا أُلْحِقَ بِبَعْضِ أَنْوَاعِهِ مِنَ الِاسْتِقْرَاءِ وَالتَّمْثِيلِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَعَكْسُهُ) أَيْ : عَكْسُ النَّظَرِيِّ . وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «العَكْسِ» هُنَا : المَعْنَى اللُّغَوِيُّ الَّذِي هُوَ مُطْلَقُ المُخَالِفِ، لَا المَعْنَى الاِصْطِلَاحِيَّ الَّذِي هُوَ: «قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ» كَمَا سَيَأْتِي، فَالضَّرُورِيُّ هُوَ : «مَا لَا يَحْتَاجُ لِتَأمُّل»؛ أَيْ: نَظَرٍ وَفِكْرِ بِالمَعْنَى المُتَقَدِّم، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يَدْخُلُ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الضَّرُورِيِّ بِمَا ذَكَرَ : القَضَايَا الأَوَّلِيَّاتُ، وَالحَدْسِيَّاتُ، وَالتَّجْرِييَّاتُ : أَمَّا الأُوْلَى فَهِيَ : القَضَايَا الَّتِي لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ التَّصْدِيقُ بِهَا عَلَى شَيْءٍ أَصْلاً، بَلْ تُصَدِّقُ النَّفْسُ بِهَا مِنْ أَوَّلِ وَهْلَةٍ ؛ أَعْنِي : بِمُجَرَّدِ الِالْتِفَاتِ إِلَيْهَا ، وَلِذَلِكَ نُسِبَتْ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ ؛ كَقَوْلِهِمْ : «الكُلُّ أَعْظَمُ مِنَ الجُزْءِ، وَالوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَيْنِ». وَأَمَّا الثَّانِيَةُ فَهِيَ : الَّتِي يَتَوَقَّفُ التَّصْدِيقُ بِهَا عَلَى حَدْسِ وَتَحْمِينِ؛ كَقَوْلِهِمْ: نُورُ القَمَرِ مُسْتَفَادٌ مِنْ نُورِ الشَّمْسِ . وَأَمَّا الثَّالِثَةُ فَهِيَ : الَّتِي يَتَوَفَّفُ التَّصْدِيقُ بِهَا عَلَى التَّجْرِبَةِ؛ كَقَوْلِهِمْ: «السَّقَمُونِيَا مُسْهِلَةٌ لِلصَّفْرَاءِ الَّتِي هِيَ إِحْدَى الطَّبَائِعِ الأَرْبَعِ، وَإِنَّمَا دَخَلَتْ هَذِهِ وَالَّتِي قَبْلَهَا فِي تَعْرِيفِ الضَّرُورِيِّ؛ لأَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا وَإِنْ تَوَقَّفَ عَلَى حَدْسٍ أَوْ تَجْرِبَةٍ لَمْ يَتَوَفَّفْ عَلَى تَأمُّلٍ وَعَلَى نَظَرٍ . قَوْلُهُ : (هُوَ الضَّرُورِيُّ) وَيُرَادِفُهُ : «البَدِيهِيُّ» عَلَى القَوْلِ بِأَنَّهُ مَا لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى نَظَرٍ وَاسْتِدْلَالٍ.
And the theoretical: that which requires reflection, and its opposite is the necessary clear one. That is: by intending by it that which leads to the unknown from definition, syllogism, induction, or analogy, not that which is specific to definition and syllogism, as might be understood from the expression 'theoretical', for the immediate understanding is that it is relative to technical reasoning only, and it is not so. Rather, it is relative to reasoning in a sense that includes the technical and what is annexed to some of its types, like induction and analogy, so let it be pondered. His statement: (and its opposite) meaning the opposite of the theoretical. And what is meant by 'opposite' here is the linguistic meaning which is absolute opposition, not the technical meaning which is 'conversion of the two parts of the proposition' as will come. So the necessary is 'that which does not require reflection', i.e., reasoning and thinking in the aforementioned sense. And it is not hidden that within the definition of the necessary by what he mentioned enter: the first principles, the intuitive propositions, and the experiential propositions. As for the first, they are propositions whose assent does not depend on anything at all, rather the soul assents to them from the first moment, I mean: merely by turning attention to them, and therefore they are attributed to the first; like their saying: 'the whole is greater than the part, and one is half of two.' As for the second, they are those whose assent depends on intuition and estimation; like their saying: 'the light of the moon is derived from the light of the sun.' As for the third, they are those whose assent depends on experience; like their saying: 'scammony is an evacuant of yellow bile, which is one of the four humors.' And these and the previous one entered into the definition of the necessary because each of them, even if it depends on intuition or experience, does not depend on reflection and reasoning. His statement: (it is the necessary) and synonymous with it is 'the self-evident' according to the view that it is what does not need reasoning and inference.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding the definition of two fundamental epistemological categories: al-nazari (theoretical) and al-daruri (necessary). The commentator clarifies that 'nazari' is not restricted to formal syllogistic reasoning (al-nazar al-istilahi) but includes induction and analogy, which are annexed to it. Then he explains that 'aks' (opposite) here is used linguistically, not technically. The necessary is what requires no reflection. Notably, the commentator includes three types of propositions under 'daruri': first principles (awwaliyat), intuitive propositions (haddsiyat), and experiential propositions (tajribiyat). Although the latter two depend on intuition or experience, they are considered necessary because they do not require deliberate reasoning (ta'amul). The passage ends by equating 'daruri' with 'badihi' (self-evident). Throughout, the commentator uses Islamic examples: the whole is greater than the part, the moon's light from the sun, and scammony's purgative effect on yellow bile.

Key Terms
النَّظَرِي
الضَّرُورِي
الأَوَّلِيَّات
الحَدْسِيَّات
التَّجْرِيبيَّات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what does the term 'nazari' include besides definition and syllogism?
A) Only definition and syllogism
B) Induction and analogy
C) First principles and intuitions
D) Experience and deduction

Key Takeaway
The theoretical (nazari) requires reflection and encompasses definition, syllogism, induction, and analogy; the necessary (daruri) requires no reflection and includes first principles, intuitions, and experiential propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following proposition as nazari or daruri according to the definitions in this lesson: 'Faith in Allah is obligatory.' Does it require reflection or is it self-evident? Justify your answer using the criteria from the passage.
?
Identify which type of necessary proposition (awwali, hadsi, or tajribi) the following statement is: 'Fire burns.' Explain your reasoning based on the commentator's classification.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
Three Types of Necessary Propositions
Author defines nazari and daruri, then enumerates three types of necessary propositions: awwaliyat, hadsiyat, tajribiyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between awwaliyat, hadsiyat, and tajribiyat and provide Islamic examples of each.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge includes immediate, intuitive, and experiential propositions.

The commentator classifies necessary propositions into three types: first principles (awwaliyat), intuitive propositions (haddsiyat), and experiential propositions (tajribiyat). This lesson unpacks each type with its defining feature and examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Locate in the passage where the commentator mentions awwaliyat, hadsiyat, and tajribiyat as types of daruri propositions.
2
Understand awwaliyat (first principles)
These are propositions whose assent does not depend on anything at all. The soul affirms them immediately upon attention. Example: 'the whole is greater than the part'.
3
Understand hadsiyat (intuitive propositions)
These depend on intuition (hads) and estimation, but not on deliberate reflection. Example: 'the light of the moon is derived from the sun'.
4
Understand tajribiyat (experiential propositions)
These depend on experience (tajriba) through repeated observation. Example: 'scammony is an evacuant of yellow bile'.
5
Justify their inclusion as daruri
The commentator explains that hadsiyat and tajribiyat are still necessary because they do not require reflection (ta'amul), even though they depend on intuition or experience.

وَالنَّظَرِيُّ : مَا احْتَاجَ لِلتَّأَمُّلِ وَعَكْسُهُ هُوَ الضَّرُورِيُّ الجَلِي. أَيْ: بِأَنْ يُرِيدُوا بِهِ مَا يُوصِلُ إِلَى المَجْهُولِ مِنْ تَعْرِيفٍ أَوْ قِيَاسٍ أَوِ اسْتِقْرَاءِ أَوْ Two Meanings of Conventional Signification
Bajuri defines two senses of dalalah wad'iyyah and discusses its scope in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of conventional signification (potential vs actual) and apply the condition of generality (ittarad) to determine what counts as signification for logicians.

Core Idea
Signification in logic requires generality — not every occasional understanding qualifies.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary clarifies that the term 'dalalah' is used in two distinct senses: potential and actual. Understanding this distinction is crucial because logicians only consider signification that is universal and consistent. You will learn how to apply the condition of ittarad to evaluate whether a given relation counts as proper dalalah in the logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses
Find the two meanings of dalalah in the passage. What is the key difference between them?
The first meaning is potential understanding; the second is actual understanding.
2
Note the objection against haythiyyat
Why might using 'bi-haythu' be problematic in definitions? What is the resolution?
The objection is that bi-haythu indicates potentiality, not actuality. The resolution is that in this context, potentiality is the focus.
3
Understand the reversal of signifier and signified
In the first meaning, the first entity is the signifier; in the second, the first entity is the signified. Why does this reversal matter?
It shows that the relation is different: one is dispositional, the other is actual.
4
Apply the generality condition
What does al-Sayyid say about the signification considered in this art? How does it differ from the approach of grammarians and usul scholars?
Al-Sayyid says signification must be universal (kulliyyah). Logicians do not consider occasional understandings as true dalalah.
5
Examine the exception for metaphor
Al-Sa'd said metaphor indicates its metaphorical meaning. Does this violate the generality condition? Why or why not?
It does not violate because metaphorical signification is also universal in its own genre, i.e., consistent for that word.

أَنْواعُ الدَّلَالَةِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ تُطْلَقُ بِالِاشْتِرَاكِ عَلَى مَعْنَيَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : كَوْنُ أَمْرٍ بِحَيْثُ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ أَمْرٌ آخَرُ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُفْهَمْ مِنْهُ بِالفِعْلِ. وَالمُرَادُ بِالأَمْرِ الأَوَّلِ : الدَّالُ، وَبِالثَّانِي : المَدْلُولُ . وَاعْتُرِضَ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفُ بِأَنَّ الحَيْثِيَّاتِ تُجْتَنَبُ فِي التَّعَارِيفِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا لَا تَدُلُّ عَلَى الحُصُولِ، وَإِنَّمَا تَدُلُّ عَلَى القَابِلِيَّةِ ; قَالَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ : وَالبَحْثُ فِيهِ مَجَالٌ . وَلَعَلَّ وَجْهَهُ : أَنَّ مَحَلَّ اجْتِنَابِهَا مَا لَمْ يَكُنِ المَدَارُ عَلَى القَابِلِيَّةِ كَمَا هُنَا . ثَانِيَهُمَا : فَهْمُ أَمْرٍ مِنْ أَمْرٍ؛ أَيْ: فَهْمُهُ مِنْهُ بِالفِعْلِ، فَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مِمَّا قَبْلَهُ، وَالمُرَادُ بِالأَمْرِ الأَوَّلِ : المَدْلُولُ، وَبِالثَّانِي : الدَّالُ، عَلَى عَكْسِ مَا قَبْلَهُ. وَيَنْبَنِي عَلَى المَعْنَيَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ : أَنَّ الأَمْرَ قَبْلَ حُصُولِ الفَهْمِ مِنْهُ بِالفِعْلِ يُقَالُ لَهُ : «دَالٌ» حَقِيقَةً عَلَى الأَوَّلِ دُونَ الثَّانِي. وَلَا بُدَّ فِي الدَّلَالَةِ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ هَذَا الفَنِّ مِنِ اطِّرَادِهَا، وَلِهَذَا قَالَ السَّيِّدُ : الدَّلَالَةُ المُعْتَبَرَةُ فِي هَذَا الفَنِّ مَا كَانَتْ كُلِّيَّةً، وَأَمَّا إِذَا فُهِمَ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ مَعْنًى فِي بَعْضِ الأَوْقَاتِ بِوَاسِطَةِ قَرِينَةٍ، فَأَهْلُ هَذَا الفَنِّ لَا يَحْكُمُونَ بِأَنَّهُ دَالٌ عَلَيْهِ بِخِلَافِ أَصْحَابِ العَرَبِيَّةِ وَالأُصُولِ . لَكِنَّ الَّذِي صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّعْدُ فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ أَنَّ المَجَازَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ المَجَازِيِّ.
Types of conventional signification. Know that 'dalalah' is used homonymously for two meanings: First: an entity being such that another entity is understood from it, even if not actually understood from it. What is meant by the first entity is the signifier, and by the second is the signified. This definition was objected to on the grounds that 'considerations' (haythiyyat) are avoided in definitions because they do not indicate actuality, but only capacity. One of the investigators said: There is room for discussion in this. Perhaps its justification is that the place of avoiding them is when the basis is not on capacity as it is here. Second: the understanding of an entity from an entity, i.e., its actual understanding from it, so it is more specific than the former. What is meant by the first entity is the signified, and by the second is the signifier, contrary to the former. And upon the two mentioned meanings it follows that the entity before the actual understanding from it is called 'signifier' in reality according to the first meaning but not the second. And in the signification considered by the practitioners of this art, it must be general. For this reason, al-Sayyid said: The signification considered in this art is what is universal. As for when a meaning is understood from a word sometimes by means of a context, the practitioners of this art do not judge that it is a signifier of it, unlike the scholars of Arabic and usul. However, al-Sa'd explicitly stated in his commentary on al-Shamsiyyah that metaphor indicates its metaphorical meaning.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the types of conventional signification (dalalah wad'iyyah). The author, Bajuri, begins by stating that the term 'dalalah' is used homonymously for two meanings. The first meaning is the potential for an entity to be understood from another, even if not actually understood. Here, the signifier (dal) is the first entity, and the signified (madlul) is the second. An objection is raised that definitions using 'considerations' (haythiyyat) are avoided because they indicate potentiality rather than actuality. The response is that in this context, the focus is indeed on potentiality, so it is acceptable. The second meaning is the actual understanding of one entity from another, which is more specific. Here the order is reversed: the first entity is the signified, the second is the signifier. Building on these two meanings, the author notes that before actual understanding, an entity is called 'signifier' truly only according to the first meaning. Then the passage discusses the condition of generality (ittarad): logicians only consider signification that is universal, i.e., consistent. If a word sometimes indicates a meaning due to a context, logicians do not consider it a signifier of that meaning, unlike grammarians and usul scholars. However, al-Sa'd clarified that metaphor does indicate its metaphorical meaning, which implies that metaphorical signification is also universal in its own way. The passage thus clarifies the scope of dalalah in logic, emphasizing the need for consistent, rule-governed signification.

وضعية
حيثية
اطّراد

B) First meaning: potential understanding; second meaning: actual understanding.
C) Both meanings are identical.
D) First meaning: understanding a part; second meaning: understanding a whole.

Key Takeaway
The two meanings of dalalah — potential and actual — and the condition of ittarad define the scope of signification in Islamic logic.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
8 min
The Three Types of Conventional Signification
Author defines three types and provides justification for classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish conformity (muṭābaqa), inclusion (taḍammun), and consequence (iltizām) in the signification of words, using the criteria of whether the signified meaning is complete, partial, or external.

Core Idea
A word signifies its full meaning, its parts, and its necessary consequences.

When you hear a word, your mind grasps meanings in layers: the full intended meaning, its component parts, and implications that necessarily follow. Logicians categorize these as conformity, inclusion, and consequence. Understanding this classification is crucial for precise definition and argumentation in Islamic sciences like uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify a word and its conventional meaning
Take a word like 'human'. Its conventional meaning is 'rational animal'. This is the complete meaning it was put for.
2
Recognize the inclusion signification
The word 'human' also signifies 'animal' because animal is part of the complete meaning. This is understood directly without a separate mental step.
Inclusion is a subset of the whole.
3
Recognize the consequence signification
The word 'human' also signifies 'capable of writing' because that necessarily follows from being rational, but is not part of the definition. This requires a mental transition from the meaning to an external property.
The consequence is not part of the original convention.
4
Distinguish by the need for transition
In conformity and inclusion, understanding is immediate. In consequence, there is a transition from the word to its meaning and then to the necessary consequent, requiring intellectual inference.
This distinction grounds the dispute about whether inclusion and consequence are conventional or intellectual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا جَعْلُهَا التِزَامِيَّةً - كَمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ - فَلَيْسَ بِشَيْءٍ؛ لِأَنَّ الفَرْدَ لَيْسَ خَارِجاً. وَوَصْفُ المُصَنِّفِ الدَّلَالَةَ بِالوَضْعِيَّةِ صَرِيحٌ فِي أَنَّ هَذِهِ الأَنْوَاعَ الثَّلَاثَةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ، وَهُوَ مَحَلُّ وفَاقٍ فِي المُطَابِقِيَّةِ، وَأَمَّا فِي التَّضَمُّنِيَّةِ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةِ فَهُوَ عَلَى الأَصَحِّ المَنْقُولِ عَنْ أَكْثَرِ المَنَاطِقَةِ، كَمَا قَالَهُ العُنَيْمِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ؛ وَوَرَاءَهُ قَوْلَانِ: أَحَدُهُمَا: أَنَّهُمَا عَقْلِيَّتَانِ، ثَانِيهُمَا: أَنَّ التَّضَمُّنِيَّةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةٌ. وَهَذِهِ إِحْدَى الطَّرِيقَتَيْنِ فِي ذَلِكَ. وَالأُخْرَى أَنَّ المُطَابِقِيَّةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ اتِّفَاقاً كَالأُولَى، وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةٌ بِلَا خِلَافٍ، وَأَمَّا التَّضَمُّنِيَّةُ فَقِيلَ: وَضْعِيَّةٌ، وَقِيلَ: عَقْلِيَّةٌ. اهـ مُلَخَّصاً مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّيْخِ المَلْوِيِّ مَعَ زِيَادَةٍ. قَوْلُهُ: (دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ) أَيِ: الوَضْعِيَّةِ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ) أَيْ: عَلَى مَعْنَىً أَوِ الَّذِي وَافَقَ ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظُ، فَـ (مَا) نَكِرَةٌ مَوْصُوفَةٌ أَوْ مَعْرِفَةٌ مَوْصُولَةٌ. وَوَجْهُ جَعْلِ التَّضَمُّنِيَّةِ وَضْعِيَّةً وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةً: أَنَّ التَّضَمُّنَ فَهْمُ الجُزْءِ فِي ضِمْنِ الكُلِّ؛ إِذْ لَا شَكَّ أَنَّهُ إِذَا فُهِمَ المَعْنَى فُهِمَتْ أَجْزَاؤُهُ. فَلَيْسَ فِيهَا انْتِقَالٌ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ إِلَى المَعْنَى، وَمِنَ المَعْنَى إِلَى الجُزْءِ، بَلْ هُوَ فَهْمٌ وَاحِدٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى تَمَامِ المَعْنَى (مُطَابَقَةً) وَبِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ (تَضَمُّناً)، بِخِلَافِ دَلَالَةِ الِالْتِزَامِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ فِيهَا مِنَ الِانْتِقَالِ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ إِلَى المَعْنَى، وَمِنَ المَعْنَى إِلَى اللَّازِمِ ضَرُورَةَ أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الوَضْعِ أَصْلاً. وَوَجْهٌ آخَرُ: بِأَنَّ الجُزْءَ دَاخِلٌ فِيمَا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ، بِخِلَافِ اللَّازِمِ، فَإِنَّهُ خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ. وَصَرَّحَ غَيْرُ وَاحِدٍ كَالْغَنِيمِيِّ بِأَنَّ الخِلَافَ لَفْظِيٌّ، فَإِنَّ مَنْ قَالَ بِعَقْلِيَّتِهِمَا لَا يُنْكِرُ أَنَّ لِلْوَضْعِ دَخْلاً فِيهِمَا، وَمَنْ قَالَ بِوَضْعِيَّتِهِمَا لَا يُنْكِرُ تَوَقُّفَهُمَا عَلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ عَقْلِيَّةٍ، فَالخِلَافُ فِي التَّسْمِيَةِ. وَفِي حَاشِيَةِ السِّيرَامِيِّ عَلَى المُطَوَّلِ: أَنَّ المَنْطِقِيِّينَ سَمَّوُا التَّضَمُّنِيَّةَ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ (وَضْعِيَّةً) وَإِنْ كَانَ لِلْعَقْلِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهِمَا؛ لِتَخْصِيصِهِمُ العَقْلِيَّةَ بِالصِّرْفَةِ، وَسَمَّاهُمَا البَيَانِيُّونَ (عَقْلِيَّةً) وَإِنْ كَانَ لِلْوَضْعِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهِمَا؛ لِعَدَمِ تَخْصِيصِهِمُ العَقْلِيَّةَ بِالصِّرْفَةِ.
As for making it [the signification of the general term to a particular individual] implicative-by-consequence—as some said—it is nothing, because the individual is not outside. And the author's description of signification as conventional is explicit that these three types are conventional, and this is a point of agreement in the case of conformity, but as for implicative-by-inclusion and implicative-by-consequence, it is according to the more correct view transmitted from most of the logicians, as al-Ghanimi and others said; and behind it are two statements: one of them: that they are both intellectual; the second: that implicative-by-inclusion is conventional and implicative-by-consequence is intellectual. And this is one of the two ways in that matter. And the other is that conformity is conventional by agreement, like the first, and implicative-by-consequence is intellectual without dispute, and as for implicative-by-inclusion, it is said: conventional, and said: intellectual. End, summarized from the commentary of Shaykh al-Mallawi with additions. His saying: 'The signification of the word' i.e., the conventional, as known from what preceded. And his saying: 'upon that which corresponds to it' i.e., upon a meaning that corresponds to that word, so 'ma' is an indefinite described or a definite relative. And the proof of making implicative-by-inclusion conventional and implicative-by-consequence intellectual: that implicative-by-inclusion is understanding the part within the whole; for there is no doubt that when the meaning is understood, its parts are understood. So there is no transition from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the part, but rather it is one understanding, called in relation to the complete meaning 'conformity' and in relation to its part 'implication-by-inclusion', unlike implicative-by-consequence, for there must be in it transition from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the necessary consequence, necessarily because the necessary consequence has no role in the convention at all. And another proof: that the part is included in what the word was put for, unlike the necessary consequence, for it is outside it. And more than one, like al-Ghanimi, stated that the dispute is verbal, for whoever says they are intellectual does not deny that convention has a role in them, and whoever says they are conventional does not deny that they depend on an intellectual premise, so the dispute is over terminology. And in the gloss of al-Sayramī on al-Mutawwal: that the logicians called implicative-by-inclusion and implicative-by-consequence 'conventional', even though reason has an entry in them, because they specialized the intellectual as pure, and the rhetoricians called them 'intellectual', even though convention has an entry in them, because they did not specialize the intellectual as pure.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical commentary on the three types of conventional signification: conformity (muṭābaqa), implication by inclusion (taḍammun), and implication by consequence (iltizām). The author begins by rejecting the opinion that the signification of a general term to a particular individual is iltizāmī, because the individual is not outside the meaning of the general term. He then clarifies that the author of the main text (al-Sullam) describes all three as conventional (waḍ‘iyya), which is agreed upon for muṭābaqa, but there is dispute regarding taḍammun and iltizām. The more correct view transmitted from most logicians, as al-Ghanimi and others state, is that both are conventional. However, there are two positions: one says both are intellectual (ʿaqliyya); the other says taḍammun is conventional and iltizām is intellectual. The latter is one of two ways; the other way is that muṭābaqa is conventional by consensus, iltizām is intellectual without dispute, and taḍammun is disputed. Then the author provides the justification for making taḍammun conventional and iltizām intellectual: in taḍammun, understanding the part occurs within the whole—when the meaning is understood, its parts are understood without a separate transition, so it is a single act of understanding. In iltizām, a transition from the word to the meaning and then to a necessary consequence is required, because the necessary consequence is not included in the convention. Another proof: the part is inside what the word was placed for, whereas the consequence is outside. Finally, it is noted that the dispute may be verbal: those who call them intellectual do not deny that convention plays a role, and those who call them conventional do not deny the need for an intellectual premise. The logicians and rhetoricians differ in how they label the non-pure case.

عقلية

Which type of signification involves a transition from the word to its meaning and then to an external property?

B) Inclusion (taḍammun)
C) Consequence (iltizām)
D) All three

Key Takeaway
The three types of conventional signification are distinguished by whether the signified meaning is the complete meaning, a part of it, or an external necessary consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
The Dispute: Conventional vs. Intellectual Signification for Inclusion and Consequence
Author presents and resolves the dispute over the classification of inclusion and consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the different scholarly positions on whether taḍammun and iltizām are conventional or intellectual, and explain the verbal nature of the dispute.

Core Idea
The disagreement over inclusion and consequence is largely terminological.

While all logicians agree that conformity is conventional, they differ over inclusion and consequence. Some consider them intellectual, others conventional, and some split the two. Interestingly, the dispute may be merely verbal: each side acknowledges a role for both convention and reason, but they label it differently. This lesson unpacks the positions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed and disputed types
Conformity is agreed to be conventional. Inclusion and consequence are disputed. Notice the author says the more correct view is that both are conventional, but there are other statements.
2
List the positions
There are two main ways: (1) Both are intellectual, or (2) inclusion is conventional and consequence is intellectual. Another way says consequence is intellectual by consensus and inclusion is disputed.
The second way has inclusion as conventional, consequence as intellectual.
3
Examine the proof for the second way
Inclusion is understanding a part within the whole without transition, so it is a single understanding; therefore it falls under the same convention. Consequence requires a mental transition to something outside the convention, so it is intellectual.
The part is included in what the word was put for; the consequence is outside.
4
Understand the verbal nature
Al-Ghanimi and others state that those who say they are intellectual do not deny convention's role, and those who say they are conventional do not deny the need for reason. The dispute is over terminology, not substance.
Logicians call them conventional because they restrict 'intellectual' to pure reason; rhetoricians call them intellectual because they do not restrict it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا جَعْلُهَا التِزَامِيَّةً - كَمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ - فَلَيْسَ بِشَيْءٍ؛ لِأَنَّ الفَرْدَ لَيْسَ خَارِجاً. وَوَصْفُ المُصَنِّفِ الدَّلَالَةَ بِالوَضْعِيَّةِ صَرِيحٌ فِي أَنَّ هَذِهِ الأَنْوَاعَ الثَّلَاثَةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ، وَهُوَ مَحَلُّ وفَاقٍ فِي المُطَابِقِيَّةِ، وَأَمَّا فِي التَّضَمُّنِيَّةِ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةِ فَهُوَ عَلَى الأَصَحِّ المَنْقُولِ عَنْ أَكْثَرِ المَنَاطِقَةِ، كَمَا قَالَهُ العُنَيْمِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ؛ وَوَرَاءَهُ قَوْلَانِ: أَحَدُهُمَا: أَنَّهُمَا عَقْلِيَّتَانِ، ثَانِيهُمَا: أَنَّ التَّضَمُّنِيَّةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةٌ. وَهَذِهِ إِحْدَى الطَّرِيقَتَيْنِ فِي ذَلِكَ. وَالأُخْرَى أَنَّ المُطَابِقِيَّةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ اتِّفَاقاً كَالأُولَى، وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةٌ بِلَا خِلَافٍ، وَأَمَّا التَّضَمُّنِيَّةُ فَقِيلَ: وَضْعِيَّةٌ، وَقِيلَ: عَقْلِيَّةٌ. اهـ مُلَخَّصاً مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّيْخِ المَلْوِيِّ مَعَ زِيَادَةٍ. قَوْلُهُ: (دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ) أَيِ: الوَضْعِيَّةِ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ) أَيْ: عَلَى مَعْنَىً أَوِ الَّذِي وَافَقَ ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظُ، فَـ (مَا) نَكِرَةٌ مَوْصُوفَةٌ أَوْ مَعْرِفَةٌ مَوْصُولَةٌ. وَوَجْهُ جَعْلِ التَّضَمُّنِيَّةِ وَضْعِيَّةً وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةً: أَنَّ التَّضَمُّنَ فَهْمُ الجُزْءِ فِي ضِمْنِ الكُلِّ؛ إِذْ لَا شَكَّ أَنَّهُ إِذَا فُهِمَ المَعْنَى فُهِمَتْ أَجْزَاؤُهُ. فَلَيْسَ فِيهَا انْتِقَالٌ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ إِلَى المَعْنَى، وَمِنَ المَعْنَى إِلَى الجُزْءِ، بَلْ هُوَ فَهْمٌ وَاحِدٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى تَمَامِ المَعْنَى (مُطَابَقَةً) وَبِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ (تَضَمُّناً)، بِخِلَافِ دَلَالَةِ الِالْتِزَامِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ فِيهَا مِنَ الِانْتِقَالِ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ إِلَى المَعْنَى، وَمِنَ المَعْنَى إِلَى اللَّازِمِ ضَرُورَةَ أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الوَضْعِ أَصْلاً. وَوَجْهٌ آخَرُ: بِأَنَّ الجُزْءَ دَاخِلٌ فِيمَا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ، بِخِلَافِ اللَّازِمِ، فَإِنَّهُ خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ. وَصَرَّحَ غَيْرُ وَاحِدٍ كَالْغَنِيمِيِّ بِأَنَّ الخِلَافَ لَفْظِيٌّ، فَإِنَّ مَنْ قَالَ بِعَقْلِيَّتِهِمَا لَا يُنْكِرُ أَنَّ لِلْوَضْعِ دَخْلاً فِيهِمَا، وَمَنْ قَالَ بِوَضْعِيَّتِهِمَا لَا يُنْكِرُ تَوَقُّفَهُمَا عَلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ عَقْلِيَّةٍ، فَالخِلَافُ فِي التَّسْمِيَةِ. وَفِي حَاشِيَةِ السِّيرَامِيِّ عَلَى المُطَوَّلِ: أَنَّ المَنْطِقِيِّينَ سَمَّوُا التَّضَمُّنِيَّةَ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ (وَضْعِيَّةً) وَإِنْ كَانَ لِلْعَقْلِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهِمَا؛ لِتَخْصِيصِهِمُ العَقْلِيَّةَ بِالصِّرْفَةِ، وَسَمَّاهُمَا البَيَانِيُّونَ (عَقْلِيَّةً) وَإِنْ كَانَ لِلْوَضْعِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهِمَا؛ لِعَدَمِ تَخْصِيصِهِمُ العَقْلِيَّةَ بِالصِّرْفَةِ.
As for making it [the signification of the general term to a particular individual] implicative-by-consequence—as some said—it is nothing, because the individual is not outside. And the author's description of signification as conventional is explicit that these three types are conventional, and this is a point of agreement in the case of conformity, but as for implicative-by-inclusion and implicative-by-consequence, it is according to the more correct view transmitted from most of the logicians, as al-Ghanimi and others said; and behind it are two statements: one of them: that they are both intellectual; the second: that implicative-by-inclusion is conventional and implicative-by-consequence is intellectual. And this is one of the two ways in that matter. And the other is that conformity is conventional by agreement, like the first, and implicative-by-consequence is intellectual without dispute, and as for implicative-by-inclusion, it is said: conventional, and said: intellectual. End, summarized from the commentary of Shaykh al-Mallawi with additions. His saying: 'The signification of the word' i.e., the conventional, as known from what preceded. And his saying: 'upon that which corresponds to it' i.e., upon a meaning that corresponds to that word, so 'ma' is an indefinite described or a definite relative. And the proof of making implicative-by-inclusion conventional and implicative-by-consequence intellectual: that implicative-by-inclusion is understanding the part within the whole; for there is no doubt that when the meaning is understood, its parts are understood. So there is no transition from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the part, but rather it is one understanding, called in relation to the complete meaning 'conformity' and in relation to its part 'implication-by-inclusion', unlike implicative-by-consequence, for there must be in it transition from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the necessary consequence, necessarily because the necessary consequence has no role in the convention at all. And another proof: that the part is included in what the word was put for, unlike the necessary consequence, for it is outside it. And more than one, like al-Ghanimi, stated that the dispute is verbal, for whoever says they are intellectual does not deny that convention has a role in them, and whoever says they are conventional does not deny that they depend on an intellectual premise, so the dispute is over terminology. And in the gloss of al-Sayramī on al-Mutawwal: that the logicians called implicative-by-inclusion and implicative-by-consequence 'conventional', even though reason has an entry in them, because they specialized the intellectual as pure, and the rhetoricians called them 'intellectual', even though convention has an entry in them, because they did not specialize the intellectual as pure.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical commentary on the three types of conventional signification: conformity (muṭābaqa), implication by inclusion (taḍammun), and implication by consequence (iltizām). The author begins by rejecting the opinion that the signification of a general term to a particular individual is iltizāmī, because the individual is not outside the meaning of the general term. He then clarifies that the author of the main text (al-Sullam) describes all three as conventional (waḍ‘iyya), which is agreed upon for muṭābaqa, but there is dispute regarding taḍammun and iltizām. The more correct view transmitted from most logicians, as al-Ghanimi and others state, is that both are conventional. However, there are two positions: one says both are intellectual (ʿaqliyya); the other says taḍammun is conventional and iltizām is intellectual. The latter is one of two ways; the other way is that muṭābaqa is conventional by consensus, iltizām is intellectual without dispute, and taḍammun is disputed. Then the author provides the justification for making taḍammun conventional and iltizām intellectual: in taḍammun, understanding the part occurs within the whole—when the meaning is understood, its parts are understood without a separate transition, so it is a single act of understanding. In iltizām, a transition from the word to the meaning and then to a necessary consequence is required, because the necessary consequence is not included in the convention. Another proof: the part is inside what the word was placed for, whereas the consequence is outside. Finally, it is noted that the dispute may be verbal: those who call them intellectual do not deny that convention plays a role, and those who call them conventional do not deny the need for an intellectual premise. The logicians and rhetoricians differ in how they label the non-pure case.

Key Terms
دلالة تضمنية
دلالة التزامية
وضعية
عقلية
خلاف لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the parties agree on the facts but differ on the name
B) Because one side is wrong and the other right
C) Because the issue is not important
D) Because the author does not care

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the classification of inclusion and consequence is terminological; both convention and reason are involved in both types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examine the following sentence: 'The word 'fire' signifies heat by consequence.' According to the logicians and rhetoricians, would this be conventional or intellectual? Explain using the verbal dispute resolution.

Dalalat al-Mutabaqa: Signification by Correspondence
Bajuri defines dalalat al-mutabaqa and answers an objection about identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dalalat al-mutabaqa and explain how the word and its meaning are considered distinct despite being identical in essence.

Core Idea
The word and its meaning are one thing, considered differently.

In this lesson, we examine the first type of verbal signification: dalalat al-mutabaqa (correspondence). The author defines it as the word's signification of its full meaning. An objection arises: if the word and meaning are the same, how can one signify the other? The answer introduces a key logical distinction between essence and consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic: دلالة المطابقة (dalalat al-mutabaqa). This is the subject of the passage.
2
Extract the definition
The definition: دلالة اللفظ على ما وافقه – the signification of a word for its entire meaning (the full concept for which the word was coined).
3
Understand the objection
The objector says that the word and its meaning are one in essence (متحدان ذاتاً), so there is no correspondence between two distinct things.
4
Analyze the answer
Bajuri responds that they are identical in essence but differ in consideration (اعتباراً). The word as subject (موضوع) is different from the meaning as signified (مدلول) by way of mental perspective.
5
Apply to an example
Consider 'human' (إنسان) which signifies 'rational animal'. The expression 'human' and the concept 'rational animal' are the same reality but considered from two angles: as a word and as a meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ يَدْعُونَهَا: «دَلَالَةَ المُطَابَقَهُ» فَإِنْ قِيلَ : إِنَّهُمَا مُتَّحِدَانِ لَا مُتَغَايَرَانِ حَتَّى يَصِحْ ذَلِكَ . أَجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُمَا وَإِنِ اتَّحَدَا ذَاتاً تَغَايَرا اعْتِبَاراً ؛ إِذِ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ بِاعْتِبَارِ كَوْنِهِ مَوْضُوعاً لَهُ غَيْرُهُ بِاعْتِبَارِ كَوْنِهِ مَدْلُولاً .
The signification of a word for what corresponds to it they call 'signification by correspondence.' If it is said: they are one, not distinct, so that is not valid. The answer: although they are one in essence, they differ in consideration; for 'rational animal' in terms of being the subject for it is different from it in terms of being the signified.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of verbal signification, called 'dalalat al-mutabaqa' (signification by correspondence). The author (Bajuri) presents a potential objection: the word (lafz) and its meaning (ma‘na) in mutabaqa are identical in essence, so how can the word 'correspond' to itself? The answer is that although they are the same in reality (dhat), they are considered from two perspectives: one is the word as a subject (mawdu‘) for that meaning, and the other is the meaning as the signified (madlul). Thus the relation of correspondence holds between two different considerations, not between two separate entities. An example from logic: the term 'rational animal' considered as the linguistic expression is the word, and considered as the concept it signifies is the meaning.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
موضوع له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Bajuri, how do the word and its meaning in dalalat al-mutabaqa relate?
A) They are two distinct entities.
B) They are identical in essence but differ in consideration.
C) They are completely separate.
D) They are related by necessary implication.

Key Takeaway
Dalalat al-mutabaqa is the direct correspondence between a word and its full meaning, distinguished by consideration not essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the term 'salat' (prayer) from fiqh. Explain why its signification of the specific prescribed actions is considered dalalat al-mutabaqa, not tadamun or iltizam.
?
Construct a similar objection-answer pair for the term 'zakat' (obligatory charity) using the same distinction between essence and consideration.
